EV Lab — Question Bank

1075 questions • generated 2026-05-26 00:49
1What is an electric vehicle?
An electric vehicle is a vehicle powered fully or partly by electricity instead of burning fuel. It uses an electric motor driven from a battery or supply line. EVs help reduce noise, local pollution, and dependence of transport on oil.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.22 • EV Basics & History
2Why are electric vehicles considered important today?
Electric vehicles matter because they reduce noise and local pollution, cut transport's dependence on oil, and can lower overall carbon-dioxide emissions when their electricity comes from non-fossil sources such as nuclear, wind or hydro power, helping the world fight climate change.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.22 • EV Basics & History
3Roughly how many years of oil supply are left at current usage rates?
The textbook gives a worst-case estimate of only about 40 years of oil supply at current usage rates. In practice, scarcity will raise prices, slowly cutting demand. Coal can also be turned into oil, with over 100 years of coal still available.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.22 • EV Basics & History
4Why is global warming linked to fossil-fuel vehicles?
Burning fossil fuels in vehicles releases carbon dioxide into the atmosphere. This carbon dioxide is widely blamed for global warming, which can cause climate change and rising sea levels that could damage or destroy many of the world's coastal cities.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.22 • EV Basics & History
5Who invented the first practical DC electric motor?
The British scientist William Sturgeon invented the first commutator-type direct-current electric motor capable of turning machinery, in 1832. His work built on Michael Faraday's earlier discoveries in 1821, which laid the foundation for modern electric motors and generators.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.23 • EV Basics & History
6Who built the first known electric locomotive, and when?
The first known electric locomotive was built in 1837 by the Scottish chemist Robert Davidson. It was powered by non-rechargeable batteries. He later built a larger locomotive that was shown at the Royal Scottish Society of Arts Exhibition in 1841.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.23 • EV Basics & History
7When and where did the first regular electric tram service begin?
The first regular electric tram service was the Gross-Lichterfelde Tramway, which began running in May 1881 in Lichterfelde, a suburb of Berlin, Germany. The tramway was produced by the German engineering company Siemens & Halske AG, an early pioneer in electric transport.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.23 • EV Basics & History
8When did the first electric street tramway open in Britain?
The first electric street tramway in Britain was the Blackpool Tramway, which opened on 29 September 1885. By the outbreak of the First World War, electric trams had become a common form of city transport in many countries around the world.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.23 • EV Basics & History
9When did trolleybuses first appear?
The trolleybus dates back to 29 April 1882, when Dr Ernst Werner ran his bus in a Berlin suburb. The world's first passenger-carrying trolleybus then started service in 1901 at Bielathal, near Dresden, Germany. Britain got trolleybuses in 1911.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.23 • EV Basics & History
10Who was Camille Jenatzy and what record did he set?
Camille Jenatzy was a Belgian racing driver. Driving an electric car called 'La Jamais Contente', he set a new land-speed record of 106 kph (about 65.9 mph). This made it the first car ever to exceed 100 kph.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.23 • EV Basics & History
11Why were early electric cars preferred to petrol cars around 1900?
Around 1900, electric cars were reliable and started instantly. Internal combustion (IC) cars were unreliable, smelly and had to be hand-cranked to start. Steam-engine cars also needed time to light their burners and had relatively low thermal efficiency, making EVs the better choice.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.25 • EV Basics & History
12How many electric vehicles were produced by the 1920s?
By the 1920s, several hundred thousand electric vehicles had already been built. They served as cars, vans, taxis, delivery vehicles and buses. After cheap oil and the IC self-starter (invented in 1911) arrived, however, petrol cars rapidly overtook them.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.25 • EV Basics & History
13What is regenerative braking?
Regenerative braking is a system where the electric motor acts as a generator while the car is slowing down. It converts the vehicle's kinetic energy back into electricity, which is returned to the battery for reuse instead of being lost as heat.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.25 • EV Basics & History
14How much energy per kilogram does petrol store compared with lead-acid battery?
Petrol stores roughly 9000 Wh per kilogram of fuel, while a lead-acid battery stores only about 30 Wh per kilogram. That is around 300 times less energy per kilogram, which is a major reason combustion engines historically beat batteries on range.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.25 • EV Basics & History
15What is the typical efficiency of a petrol engine and gearbox?
A typical petrol engine plus gearbox and transmission has an overall efficiency of about 20%. That means only one-fifth of the fuel's chemical energy becomes useful motion at the shaft, while most of the rest is lost as heat through exhaust and cooling.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.25 • EV Basics & History
16How efficient is an electric motor in turning battery energy into motion?
An electric motor is highly efficient, typically converting about 90% of the electrical energy from the battery into useful motion at the motor shaft. That is much higher than a petrol engine's roughly 20%, which is one of the biggest advantages of EVs.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.25 • EV Basics & History
17Why is recharging time a big issue for battery vehicles?
Filling a petrol tank takes about a minute, but recharging a lead-acid battery normally takes several hours, even with sufficient power supply. Newer batteries have cut this to under an hour, but it is still much slower than refuelling a petrol car.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.26 • EV Basics & History
18Who built the first hybrid vehicle?
Ferdinand Porsche developed an early hybrid vehicle in 1900. His design could run on electricity from a battery or use a 2.5-horsepower Daimler petrol engine to drive a generator. This combined the strengths of both power sources in a single car.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.27 • EV Basics & History
19Why do railway companies prefer electric trains?
Electric trains require less maintenance, both of the locomotive itself and of the track. They are also clean at the point of use and have developed steadily through the twentieth century using both DC and AC systems, making them the preferred option for rail operators.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.28 • EV Basics & History
20Where are electric vehicles ideal because of their quiet, clean operation?
Electric vehicles produce no exhaust emissions where they are driven and are inherently quiet. That makes them ideal for warehouses, inside buildings, and on golf courses, where pollution from exhaust gases and engine noise would not be tolerated by people working nearby.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.28 • EV Basics & History
21What is one of the most common everyday uses of electric vehicles in Europe and the USA?
A very common use of battery electric vehicles in Europe and the USA is mobility devices for elderly and physically handicapped users. They can be driven on pavements, into shops and inside buildings, with a typical range of about 4 miles being enough.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.28 • EV Basics & History
22Why are EVs efficient in start-stop driving?
Electric vehicles keep their efficiency in start-stop driving, unlike IC engines which become very inefficient and polluting in stop-and-go conditions. This made EVs attractive for delivery work — the British 'milk float' is a famous historical example of this practical strength.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.29 • EV Basics & History
23What is the Shinkansen, and when did it start running?
The Shinkansen is Japan's bullet train, a high-speed electric train. The first Tokaido Shinkansen line began service in 1964 between Tokyo and Osaka, covering about 515 km in around four hours. Modern Shinkansen trains reach speeds of around 300 kph.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.29 • EV Basics & History
24What is a maglev train?
A maglev (magnetic levitation) train does not use wheels. It is magnetically levitated above a special track and pushed forward by linear electric motors. The experimental Japanese JR-Maglev reached 581 kph (361 mph), the current world record for a crewed train.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.29 • EV Basics & History
25What recent advance enabled the modern wave of electric cars?
The biggest recent advance has been the lithium battery. It offers a reasonable specific energy and a much shorter charging time than older batteries. This made vehicles like the Tesla sports car, Nissan Leaf and Mitsubishi MiEV commercially practical.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.30 • EV Basics & History
26What kind of hybrid is the Toyota Prius?
The Toyota Prius is a hybrid vehicle that cannot be recharged from the mains. It is efficient, but it still relies entirely on oil because it cannot draw electricity from the grid, which may be generated from a wide range of cleaner sources.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.32 • EV Basics & History
27What kind of vehicle is the Chevrolet Volt?
The Chevrolet Volt is a rechargeable (plug-in) hybrid released by General Motors. It can complete many journeys using only electricity, and uses its internal combustion engine as a 'range extender' to keep going when the battery runs low on longer trips.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.32 • EV Basics & History
28Why might a fuel-cell vehicle have an advantage over a battery EV?
A fuel-cell vehicle can give a much longer range than a battery EV. It normally runs on hydrogen, which is stored on board the vehicle. Honda's FCX Clarity is an example of a hydrogen fuel-cell car leased to customers in the USA.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.33 • EV Basics & History
29How fast did electric two-wheeler sales grow in China?
Annual sales of electric bicycles and scooters in China grew from about 56,000 in 1998 to over 21 million in 2008. For comparison, China sold around 9.4 million automobiles in the same year, showing the huge popularity of two-wheeled EVs there.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.33 • EV Basics & History
30What are the three main environmental benefits of electric vehicles?
Electric vehicles offer three main environmental benefits: reducing local pollution from the vehicles themselves, reducing dependence on oil and other fossil fuels, and reducing overall carbon-dioxide emissions, especially when the electricity used comes from cleaner sources such as nuclear or renewables.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.34 • EV Basics & History
31How does shifting from planes to electric trains save energy?
Electric trains use only a fraction of the energy per passenger-mile compared with aircraft. So encouraging people to take high-speed electric trains instead of flying for shorter trips can lead to large energy savings and reductions in carbon emissions overall.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.35 • EV Basics & History
32What share of Britain's electricity came from low-carbon sources at the time of writing?
At the time of writing, around 20% of Britain's electricity was generated by nuclear power, with about another 10% coming from alternative sources such as wind and hydro. So roughly 30% of UK electricity came from low-carbon sources.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.35 • EV Basics & History
33What share of France's electricity in 2003 was carbon-free?
In 2003, around 90% of France's electricity generation released no carbon. About 75% was generated by nuclear power and a further 15% by hydro, while only about 10% came from fossil-fuel power stations — making French EVs unusually clean.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.35 • EV Basics & History
34What is California's Low Emission Vehicle Program?
California's Low Emission Vehicle Program is a set of regulations that encouraged motor manufacturers to produce electric vehicles and pushed for the introduction of zero-emission cars. The complex rules led to important developments in fuel-cell, battery and hybrid electric vehicle technology.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.36 • EV Basics & History
35What does 'tail-pipe emissions' mean?
Tail-pipe emissions are the exhaust gases that come out of a vehicle's exhaust pipe — the gases produced by burning fuel in the engine. Electric vehicles have no tail-pipe emissions; any emissions are produced at the power station, which can control them better.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.36 • EV Basics & History
36Why did petrol cars overtake electric cars in the early 20th century?
Around 1900 electric cars were more reliable than petrol cars, but the 1911 invention of the IC self-starter and the arrival of cheap oil tipped the balance. Heavy, expensive batteries with slow recharging and short range made EVs less practical for most drivers.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.25 • EV Basics & History
37How much battery mass would be needed to match 45 litres of petrol?
To match the useful energy from 45 litres (10 gallons) of petrol, the textbook says you would need a staggering 2.7 tonnes of lead-acid batteries. That huge mass is exactly why early battery EVs could not compete on long-distance range.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.25 • EV Basics & History
38Why does owning a battery EV stay expensive over time?
Batteries are expensive when new, so an EV usually costs more than a similar petrol car. Batteries also have a limited life, typically about five years. Owners therefore must make further large investments to replace the battery pack periodically.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.27 • EV Basics & History
39Why are high-speed electric trains becoming a real alternative to flying?
High-speed electric trains travel city centre to city centre, load faster than aircraft, and use only a fraction of the energy per passenger. In countries with low-carbon grids like France, they also avoid fossil fuels altogether, producing no CO2 emissions in use.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.30 • EV Basics & History
40How do typical US driving distances make a 100-mile-range EV practical?
In the USA, about 50% of journeys are under 25 miles, 75% under 40 miles, and 94% under 100 miles. So a modern EV like the Nissan Leaf with about 100 miles of range can comfortably cover the vast majority of typical daily trips.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.36 • EV Basics & History
41Why is regenerative braking valued for reasons beyond saving energy?
After all the losses through the motor, controller and battery, less than a third of the braking kinetic energy is recovered. So regenerative braking is valued as much as a convenient way of slowing down heavy electric cars as for the modest energy savings.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.25 • EV Basics & History
42Why doesn't a plug-in hybrid need recharging on every trip?
A plug-in hybrid like the Chevrolet Volt has an electric-only range of about 40 miles, which already covers roughly 75% of typical journeys. For longer drives, its onboard petrol engine acts as a range extender, so the car never needs to stop to charge.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.36 • EV Basics & History
43How do EVs help city air quality even with a coal-heavy grid?
Even when grid electricity comes from fossil fuels, EVs remove tail-pipe pollution from busy city streets and shift those emissions to power stations, where they can be controlled. So cities still see cleaner air and quieter roads, even before the grid gets greener.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.36 • EV Basics & History
44Why is a Toyota Prius less environmentally clean than a plug-in hybrid?
The Toyota Prius is a non-rechargeable hybrid, so ultimately all its energy comes from petrol. A plug-in hybrid like the Chevrolet Volt can also draw electricity from the mains, and that electricity may come from cleaner sources such as nuclear or wind.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.32 • EV Basics & History
45Why don't trams and trolleybuses suffer from range anxiety?
Trams, trolleybuses and electric trains take their power directly from overhead lines or supply rails along their route, rather than from on-board batteries. So they never run out of stored energy and never need to be recharged or refuelled in service.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.36 • EV Basics & History
46Using textbook numbers, how does shaft energy from 4 kg of petrol compare with 270 kg of lead-acid battery?
A 4 kg petrol tank (4.5 L) holds about 36,000 Wh of energy; at 20% engine plus gearbox efficiency, that gives about 7,200 Wh of useful shaft energy. A 270 kg lead-acid pack stores about 8,100 Wh; at 90% motor efficiency, also about 7,200 Wh.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.27 • EV Basics & History
47Why is doubling EV range much harder than doubling petrol-car range?
Doubling a petrol car's range needs only about 4 extra kilograms of fuel, but doubling a lead-acid EV's range needs another 270 kg of batteries. The extra mass itself uses energy to accelerate, decelerate and climb hills, eating into the range gained.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.25 • EV Basics & History
48Why is the recovery rate from regenerative braking actually quite low?
Each stage of regenerative braking loses some energy: the motor acting as generator, the controller, the battery storing the energy, then the controller and motor again on reuse. After all these losses, in practice less than a third of the kinetic energy is recovered.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.25 • EV Basics & History
49Why does an EV's environmental benefit depend so much on the local grid mix?
Most EV emissions are shifted from the road to the power station. In France, where about 90% of electricity is low-carbon, EV charging is very clean. In a coal-heavy grid, well-to-wheel CO2 is much higher, so the same EV is greener in one country than another.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.35 • EV Basics & History
50Why did the arrival of lithium batteries trigger the modern EV revival?
Lithium batteries store much more energy per kilogram than lead-acid and can be recharged in a shorter time. That combination made longer driving range and shorter charging stops practical, enabling commercial cars such as the Tesla sports car, Nissan Leaf and Mitsubishi MiEV.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.30 • EV Basics & History
51What are the three main parts of a battery electric vehicle?
A battery electric vehicle consists of three core parts: an electric battery for energy storage, an electric motor to drive the wheels, and a controller that manages the power going from the battery to the motor, deciding how fast and in which direction the vehicle moves.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.38 • Types of EVs
52How is a battery EV's battery recharged?
A battery EV's battery is normally recharged from mains electricity. The car is connected via a plug to a battery charging unit, which can either be carried on board the vehicle itself or fitted permanently at the charging point or station.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.38 • Types of EVs
53What is a two-quadrant controller in an EV?
A two-quadrant controller lets an electric vehicle move forwards and backwards. It manages the power supplied to the motor, controlling vehicle speed in both directions. It is called 'two-quadrant' because it handles only the two driving directions, without controlled regenerative braking.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.38 • Types of EVs
54What is a four-quadrant controller?
A four-quadrant controller adds regenerative braking in both directions to a two-quadrant design. It can drive the vehicle forwards and backwards and also brake regeneratively in either direction, recovering some kinetic energy back into the battery instead of wasting it as heat.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.38 • Types of EVs
55What is a hybrid vehicle?
A hybrid vehicle is one that has two or more sources of power. The most common type combines an internal combustion engine with a battery and an electric motor (which can also act as a generator). This gives the car flexibility in how power is delivered.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.38 • Types of EVs
56What are the two basic arrangements for hybrid vehicles?
There are two basic arrangements for hybrid vehicles: the series hybrid and the parallel hybrid. They differ in how the IC engine and the electric motor are linked to the wheels. Each has its own advantages depending on how the vehicle is used.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.38 • Types of EVs
57How does a series hybrid drive the wheels?
In a series hybrid, only the electric motor drives the wheels. The IC engine never directly powers the wheels; instead it spins a generator that produces electricity. This electricity then either goes to the battery or directly powers the drive motor that turns the wheels.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.38 • Types of EVs
58How does a parallel hybrid drive the wheels?
In a parallel hybrid, both the IC engine and the electric motor can drive the wheels. The engine can drive directly through a transmission, the electric motor can drive through the transmission or directly, or the two can drive the wheels together at the same time.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.38 • Types of EVs
59What is a non-rechargeable hybrid?
A non-rechargeable hybrid cannot be plugged in to recharge from the mains. All its energy ultimately comes from the petrol it carries; the battery is only ever charged from the IC engine while driving. So it always runs entirely from fossil fuel.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.39 • Types of EVs
60What is a rechargeable (plug-in) hybrid?
A rechargeable hybrid has a much larger battery than a non-rechargeable hybrid, large enough to give the vehicle a useful electric-only range. The battery can be recharged from the mains when the car is parked, in addition to being charged by the IC engine while driving.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.39 • Types of EVs
61Can hybrid vehicles use regenerative braking?
Yes. Both series and parallel hybrid designs allow regenerative braking. The drive motor temporarily works as a generator, which simultaneously slows the vehicle and converts some of its kinetic energy back into electricity to recharge the battery, instead of losing it as heat.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.39 • Types of EVs
62Where is the series hybrid traditionally used?
The series hybrid is traditionally used in specialist applications. The diesel-powered railway engine is nearly always a series hybrid, as are some ships. Some special all-terrain vehicles use a series hybrid layout with a separately controlled electric motor in each wheel.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.40 • Types of EVs
63What is the main disadvantage of a series hybrid?
The main disadvantage of the series hybrid is that power from the engine cannot be transmitted mechanically to the wheels. Instead, every bit of engine power must pass through both the generator and the electric motor, with losses at each stage of conversion.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.40 • Types of EVs
64Why are electric machines smaller in a parallel hybrid?
In a parallel hybrid the electric machines do not have to convert all of the vehicle's energy, because the engine can still power the wheels directly. So the motor and generator can be much smaller and cheaper than they would need to be in a series hybrid.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.40 • Types of EVs
65What is the 'degree of hybridisation'?
The degree of hybridisation is a variable used to describe how much of a parallel hybrid's power comes from its electric system versus its IC engine. A greater degree of hybridisation lets the car use a smaller IC engine running near its optimum efficiency more often.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.40 • Types of EVs
66What kind of battery does the Toyota Prius use?
The Toyota Prius uses a nickel-metal hydride (NiMH) battery. It is a non-rechargeable hybrid; the battery is only charged from the engine while driving. The car cannot be plugged in, so it is refuelled with petrol only, the conventional way.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.40 • Types of EVs
67What is the Toyota Prius's fuel economy?
The Toyota Prius has a high overall fuel economy of about 56.5 mpg (US gallons) or 68 mpg (UK gallons). Its top speed is 160 kph (100 mph), and it can accelerate from 0 to 100 kph in about 13.4 seconds.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.40 • Types of EVs
68Why does the Prius use the electric motor at start-up and low speeds?
At start-up and low speeds, the Toyota Prius runs solely on its electric motor. This avoids using the IC engine when it is least efficient and most polluting, which improves fuel economy and reduces emissions during the kinds of stop-and-go driving common in towns.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.40 • Types of EVs
69Why is the Honda Insight cited as a good example of a parallel hybrid?
The Honda Insight is given as a good example of a true parallel hybrid vehicle. Like other parallel hybrids, it allows both the IC engine and the electric motor to drive the wheels, either separately or together, sharing the work between the two power sources.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.42 • Types of EVs
70How far can the Chevrolet Volt drive on battery alone?
The Chevrolet Volt can run for up to about 40 miles (64 km) on its battery alone before the IC engine needs to start. It carries a larger battery than non-rechargeable hybrids and can be recharged from the mains while not in use.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.42 • Types of EVs
71What is a fuelled EV?
A fuelled EV works on the same basic principle as a battery EV, but the rechargeable electric battery is replaced by a fuel cell or by a metal-air battery that runs on a chemical fuel. The fuel is consumed during operation and refilled, like petrol.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.42 • Types of EVs
72When were fuel cells invented?
Fuel cells were invented in about 1840, so the basic technology has existed for nearly two centuries. Even so, they are still an unfamiliar technology for most people today, and full details of their operation are discussed in Chapter 5 of the textbook.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.42 • Types of EVs
73What is the main fuel for fuel cells in EVs?
The main fuel for fuel cells used in EVs is hydrogen. The hydrogen can be stored directly on board the vehicle, though this is not easy. An alternative is to make hydrogen on board from a liquid fuel such as methanol using an onboard reformer.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.42 • Types of EVs
74Where does the Necar 5 fit among the types of EVs?
The Necar 5 is a fuel-cell vehicle that carries an onboard methanol reformer. It can be refuelled with methanol the same way a normal car is filled with petrol. The Necar 5 has a top speed of 150 kph and uses about 5 litres of methanol per 100 km.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.42 • Types of EVs
75Why are buses a promising early use of fuel cells?
Buses are a very promising early application of fuel cells because they only refuel at one place, usually their depot. This makes the special infrastructure needed for novel fuels like hydrogen much easier to provide, since you don't need a station on every street.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.42 • Types of EVs
76How does a zinc-air battery fit into the EV power-source landscape?
A zinc-air battery is a kind of metal-air battery in which zinc electrodes react with oxygen from air to produce electricity. It is a variation on a fuel cell, and the zinc electrodes are recycled after being consumed, with the EFTC trialling them in zero-emission buses.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.42 • Types of EVs
77Where do metal-air batteries fit among the types of EV power sources?
Metal-air batteries are a variation on the fuel cell. They are refuelled by physically replacing the metal electrodes, which can then be recycled. Zinc-air batteries are a particularly promising battery in this class and have been tested in buses in the USA and Europe.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.42 • Types of EVs
78How are trolleybuses and trams powered?
Trolleybuses and trams take their power from overhead electric supply lines along their route. The trolleybus usually also has a small battery so it can travel a short distance off-wire. Both were once widely used as zero-emission city transport in many cities.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.43 • Types of EVs
79Why were many trams and trolleybuses withdrawn from service?
When trams and trolleybuses were withdrawn, cost mattered more than environmental concerns. Fossil fuel was cheap, overhead wires were thought unsightly, inflexible, expensive and a maintenance burden, and trams were seen as impeding private cars in busy streets, so cities ripped them out.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.43 • Types of EVs
80What are flywheels and supercapacitors best at?
Flywheels and supercapacitors both have a high specific power. That means they can take in and release energy very quickly. The amount of energy they store is still rather small, so they have good power density but poor energy density compared with batteries.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.43 • Types of EVs
81How does the Parry flywheel tram work?
The Parry tram stores energy in a flywheel. While the tram stops at a station, an electric motor spins the flywheel up to speed. The tram is then driven from the flywheel through a variable cone-and-ball gearbox; braking transfers kinetic energy back into the flywheel.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.43 • Types of EVs
82How are ultracapacitors used in the MAN hybrid bus?
In the MAN bus, the main power comes from a diesel engine. An ultracapacitor sits alongside it to recover kinetic energy when the bus slows down and to provide extra peak power when accelerating. This lets the bus use a smaller engine or fuel cell.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.43 • Types of EVs
83What is a solar-powered vehicle?
A solar-powered vehicle uses photovoltaic cells on its body to convert sunlight directly into electricity for the motor. The Honda Dream, which won the 1996 World Solar Challenge, crossed Australia averaging about 85 kph. Such cars are expensive and only work in very sunny conditions.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.44 • Types of EVs
84What is a linear motor?
A linear motor is essentially an electric motor that has been 'unrolled'. Instead of producing rotary motion as a torque, its unrolled stator and rotor produce a straight-line force along their length, making it useful for systems like maglev trains which travel along a track.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.45 • Types of EVs
85Where are linear motors most important in transport today?
Linear motors are most important today in maglev trains. Combined with magnetic levitation, they propel a train forward along a special track without wheels. Japan's experimental JR-Maglev train uses linear motors and has reached speeds of about 581 kph.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.45 • Types of EVs
86Why might a parallel hybrid let you use a smaller IC engine?
In a parallel hybrid, the electric motor and battery can supply peak demand bursts during acceleration. So the IC engine only needs to supply the base load, typically around 50% of peak. That lets the engine be smaller and run closer to its most efficient operating point.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.40 • Types of EVs
87Why does a diesel locomotive use a series hybrid layout?
A diesel locomotive uses a series hybrid because a mechanical drive between a powerful diesel and the wheels would be very heavy and inefficient. Instead, the diesel spins a generator and electric motors at each axle, giving smooth, controllable torque without complicated transmissions or gearboxes.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.40 • Types of EVs
88Why is the Toyota Prius not a 'pure' parallel hybrid?
The Prius mostly behaves as a parallel hybrid because its IC engine can drive the wheels directly. But it also has a separate motor and generator and a complex epicyclic 'power-splitter' gearbox, so it can also operate in series mode at times — making it a mixed design.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.42 • Types of EVs
89Why is the Honda FCX Clarity sometimes called a fuel-cell battery hybrid?
The Honda FCX Clarity uses a hydrogen fuel cell together with a lithium-ion battery in the same vehicle. Because energy comes from two different electric sources — the fuel cell and the battery, working in combination — the car can also be described as a fuel-cell battery hybrid.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.42 • Types of EVs
90Why might trams and trolleybuses make sense again today?
Today IC cars clog streets and pollute the air, so the priorities have changed. The textbook argues that EVs powered by overhead supply lines, like trams and trolleybuses, could once again make a useful contribution to clean urban transport, and shouldn't be overlooked by designers.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.43 • Types of EVs
91Why is a flywheel-only car not practical for long journeys?
A flywheel has high specific power — it can absorb and deliver energy quickly — but its energy density is poor. So it cannot store enough energy for a long journey. That is why flywheels are mostly used to assist hybrids rather than as the only source.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.43 • Types of EVs
92Why isn't the Honda Dream solar car practical for everyday use?
Solar cars like the Honda Dream are expensive and only work effectively in regions with intense sunshine. Their PV cells cannot capture enough energy in cloudy or short-day conditions for normal commuting. More realistically, solar cells can be used to top up a battery EV's charge.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.44 • Types of EVs
93Why are there so many possible hybrid combinations?
Possible hybrid energy sources include rechargeable batteries, fuel cells, solar cells, IC engines, supply lines, flywheels and capacitors. Any two of these can be paired to make a hybrid, giving over 21 two-source combinations alone, with even more options when three or more are combined.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.43 • Types of EVs
94Why does the Volt's IC engine sometimes engage at 30–70 mph?
At 30–70 mph, when the Volt's battery is below its charge threshold, the control system sometimes clutches the IC engine into the drive through an output-split planetary gearset. This lets the engine help the traction motor directly, improving high-speed performance and efficiency.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.42 • Types of EVs
95Why is the Volt sometimes described as three vehicles in one?
Depending on its battery state of charge and operating conditions, the Volt can run as a pure electric vehicle, a series hybrid, or a parallel hybrid. The control system picks whichever drive mode is most efficient at the time, so the car effectively switches identities.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.42 • Types of EVs
96Compare series and parallel hybrids on efficiency.
A series hybrid sends all engine power through a generator and then through electric motors, with losses at each step. A parallel hybrid lets the engine power the wheels mechanically when convenient, with the electric motor only supplementing it, so total energy losses can be lower.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.40 • Types of EVs
97What mechanical components do hybrids let you save?
In the series arrangement, hybrids let you remove the gearbox; the transmission can be simplified and the differential can be eliminated by using a pair of motors on opposite wheels. In both series and parallel layouts, the conventional battery-starter arrangement can also be eliminated.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.40 • Types of EVs
98Why is the Prius's epicyclic 'power-splitter' gearbox important?
The Prius's power-splitter epicyclic gearbox lets power from the electric motor and the IC engine be sent to the wheels in almost any proportion. It also routes power from the wheels to the generator during regenerative braking, giving the system great flexibility in choosing the best mix.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.42 • Types of EVs
99Why do flywheels and supercapacitors suit hybrids but not stand-alone EVs?
Flywheels and supercapacitors can absorb and release energy quickly, perfect for handling braking energy and acceleration bursts in a hybrid. But their energy density is poor, so they cannot store enough energy for a long journey on their own, ruling out a stand-alone EV.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.43 • Types of EVs
100Could solar cells be useful on an ordinary EV even though solar cars aren't practical?
Yes. Although a pure solar car like the Honda Dream is impractical for daily use, the textbook says it is perfectly feasible to wrap PV cells on a commuter EV's surface to keep the battery topped up. Falling solar costs may make this practical in future.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.44 • Types of EVs
101What is a battery in an EV?
A battery is a set of two or more electric cells connected together. The cells convert chemical energy directly into electrical energy. In a classical EV the battery is the only energy store and is the part with the highest cost, weight and volume on the whole vehicle.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.45 • Batteries — How They Work
102What makes a battery cell produce electricity?
A cell has positive and negative electrodes sitting in a liquid called the electrolyte. A chemical reaction between the electrodes and the electrolyte releases electrons, which produces direct-current (DC) electricity when the cell is connected into a circuit.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.45 • Batteries — How They Work
103What is a rechargeable (secondary) battery?
A secondary or rechargeable battery is one whose chemical reaction can be reversed. By forcing current through it in the opposite direction during charging, the battery is returned to its charged state, ready to deliver electricity again. Lead-acid is the classic example.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.45 • Batteries — How They Work
104What is internal resistance in a battery?
Internal resistance is the small built-in resistance every battery has. When current flows out, part of the battery's voltage drops across this resistance, so the terminal voltage you measure is lower than the open-circuit voltage. The lower the internal resistance, the better for EVs.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.48 • Batteries — How They Work
105What is open-circuit voltage?
Open-circuit voltage is the voltage across a battery's terminals when no current is flowing. It is the battery's 'fixed' internal voltage with no load connected. As soon as current flows, the terminal voltage drops below this value because of the internal resistance.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.48 • Batteries — How They Work
106What is the unit used to express battery capacity?
Battery capacity is given in amp-hours (Ah). One amp-hour is the charge a battery delivers when one amp flows for one hour. A 10 Ah battery, for example, could in principle supply one amp for ten hours, or two amps for five hours.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.48 • Batteries — How They Work
107Why does battery capacity drop if you draw current quickly?
When current is drawn quickly, unwanted side reactions inside the cell take place faster. So less of the stored charge becomes useful electricity. For example, a nominally 100 Ah battery may give only about 70 Ah if discharged in one hour, but 110 Ah if discharged over 20 hours.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.48 • Batteries — How They Work
108What does '2C' or '0.4C' mean in battery talk?
C stands for the battery's capacity in amphours. Saying '2C' means a current equal to twice the amphour value, and '0.4C' means 0.4 times it. For a 42 Ah battery, 2C is 84 A and 0.4C is 16.8 A. It scales current to battery size.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.48 • Batteries — How They Work
109What is specific energy in a battery?
Specific energy is the amount of electrical energy a battery stores per kilogram of its mass, measured in watt-hours per kilogram (Wh/kg). A higher specific energy means more energy for the same weight — directly meaning a longer driving range for the EV.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.50 • Batteries — How They Work
110What is energy density in a battery?
Energy density is the amount of electrical energy a battery stores per cubic metre (or per litre) of its volume, measured in Wh/m³ or Wh/L. A higher energy density means the battery takes up less space inside the car for the same stored energy.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.50 • Batteries — How They Work
111What is specific power?
Specific power is the amount of power a battery can deliver per kilogram of its mass, in watts per kilogram (W/kg). It tells you how fast the battery can release its energy — useful for hard acceleration. Batteries with high specific power often have lower specific energy.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.51 • Batteries — How They Work
112What is a Ragone plot?
A Ragone plot is a graph of specific power against specific energy on logarithmic axes. It shows how a battery's specific energy drops as specific power rises. It is used to compare different battery types and other energy sources fairly across many operating points.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.51 • Batteries — How They Work
113What is amphour (charge) efficiency?
Amphour efficiency is the ratio of the charge a battery gives back to the charge put into it during charging. Real batteries are always below 100% efficient; the efficiency depends on battery type, temperature, charging rate and the battery's state of charge.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.51 • Batteries — How They Work
114What is energy efficiency of a battery?
Energy efficiency is the ratio of the electrical energy a battery delivers when discharging to the energy needed to recharge it to the same state. Faster charging and discharging both cut this efficiency. High energy efficiency is one of the key reasons electric vehicles save energy overall.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.51 • Batteries — How They Work
115What is self-discharge in a battery?
Self-discharge is when a battery slowly loses its charge by itself, even when left unused. The rate depends on the battery type and on temperature — higher temperatures greatly speed up self-discharge. Some batteries cannot be left for long periods without recharging.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.51 • Batteries — How They Work
116What are deep cycles in a battery?
A deep cycle is a discharge that takes a battery down to a low state of charge, such as 20%, then back up to full again. Most rechargeable batteries will only survive a few hundred deep cycles. This number reflects battery lifetime and EV running costs.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.51 • Batteries — How They Work
117What is a lead-acid battery made from?
In a lead-acid cell, the negative plate is spongy lead, the positive plate is lead dioxide, and the electrolyte is dilute sulfuric acid. During discharge, both plates form lead sulfate and the electrolyte becomes more dilute. Charging reverses the reactions and restores the acid.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.55 • Batteries — How They Work
118What is a VRLA battery?
VRLA stands for valve-regulated sealed lead-acid. The battery is sealed and uses a gel electrolyte, so there is no need to top up with water. A small valve releases gas only if pressure builds up, making it essentially maintenance-free under normal use.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.57 • Batteries — How They Work
119What is sulfation in a lead-acid battery?
Sulfation happens when a lead-acid battery is left in a discharged state for two weeks or more. The lead sulfate on the plates forms into large crystals that are hard to convert back into lead or lead dioxide, and they coat the plates with an insulating layer.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.57 • Batteries — How They Work
120How many cycles does a typical lead-acid EV battery last?
A lead-acid traction battery in an electric road car lasts about 700 cycles, or roughly five years. Industrial trucks like fork-lifts can get 1200–1500 cycles over 7–8 years, because their batteries are drained more slowly (7–8 hours instead of about 2 hours in cars).
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.57 • Batteries — How They Work
121What is a NiCad battery?
The nickel-cadmium (NiCad) battery uses nickel oxyhydroxide for the positive electrode and metallic cadmium for the negative electrode. It has roughly twice the specific energy of lead-acid, around 2500 cycles of life, and works from −40 to 80°C. Each cell gives a nominal 1.2 V.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.59 • Batteries — How They Work
122Why is cadmium a problem in NiCad batteries?
Cadmium is expensive — several times the cost of lead — and is unlikely to get cheaper. It is also environmentally harmful and carcinogenic. Together, these drawbacks make NiCad batteries pricier and less attractive than the cadmium-free NiMH alternative.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.59 • Batteries — How They Work
123What is a nickel-metal hydride (NiMH) battery?
A NiMH battery is similar in performance to a NiCad battery, but the negative electrode stores hydrogen in a metal hydride instead of using cadmium. It avoids the toxic cadmium problem, has specific energy around 65 Wh/kg, and can be charged in about one hour.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.61 • Batteries — How They Work
124Why does a NiMH battery often have cooling fans?
NiMH batteries can be charged so quickly that they get hot. Besides the normal heat from internal resistance, the reaction in which hydrogen bonds to the metal electrode is strongly exothermic. So most NiMH packs for EVs include integral cooling fans to keep temperatures safe.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.62 • Batteries — How They Work
125What temperature does a sodium sulfur battery run at?
Sodium sulfur batteries operate at very high temperatures, between 300°C and 350°C. They use molten sodium for the negative electrode and molten sulfur for the positive electrode, with a solid beta-alumina ceramic acting as both electrolyte and barrier between the two.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.64 • Batteries — How They Work
126What is a ZEBRA (sodium metal chloride) battery?
The ZEBRA battery uses solid nickel chloride as the positive electrode and molten sodium as the negative electrode, working around 320°C. It has very high specific energy and is much safer than the sodium sulfur battery, even passing rigorous crash tests at 50 kph.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.65 • Batteries — How They Work
127Why must a ZEBRA battery be kept hot when parked?
The ZEBRA battery only works at around 320°C, so it must stay hot or it will solidify. Unless the car is parked only briefly, the battery needs to stay plugged in to a mains supply when not in use, just to keep itself at operating temperature.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.65 • Batteries — How They Work
128What is a lithium-ion battery?
A lithium-ion battery is a rechargeable battery in which lithium ions move from the negative electrode (usually carbon/graphite) to the positive electrode (a metal oxide) during discharge, and back during charging. The flow of electrons through the external circuit produces useful electric current.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.69 • Batteries — How They Work
129What materials are inside a typical Li-ion cell?
The three main parts of a lithium-ion cell are the anode (commonly graphite carbon), the cathode (a metal oxide such as lithium cobalt oxide, lithium iron phosphate, or lithium manganese oxide), and the electrolyte (a lithium salt dissolved in an organic solvent).
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.69 • Batteries — How They Work
130Why must Li-ion charging voltage be controlled precisely?
Lithium-ion cells need accurate voltage control while charging. If the charging voltage is slightly too high, the battery can be damaged; if it is too low, the battery will not become fully charged. So suitable, smart commercial chargers are essential for Li-ion EV packs.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.69 • Batteries — How They Work
131What is a metal-air battery?
A metal-air battery cannot be recharged simply by reversing the current. Instead, the spent metal electrodes are physically replaced and the electrolyte refreshed. The used material is then sent to a reprocessing plant where it is recovered for new electrodes, much like fuel.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.69 • Batteries — How They Work
132What is a zinc-air battery?
A zinc-air battery has a solid zinc negative electrode, a porous positive electrode where oxygen from the air reacts, and a liquid alkaline electrolyte. It has a much higher specific power than the aluminium-air battery, which makes it suitable for use in road vehicles such as buses.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.70 • Batteries — How They Work
133What is a supercapacitor?
A supercapacitor (or ultracapacitor) is a very large capacitor used as an energy store. It has high specific power but low specific energy — meaning it can absorb or release energy very quickly but cannot store large amounts. A 4000 F unit can fit inside a beer-can-sized container.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.71 • Batteries — How They Work
134Why do supercapacitor banks need charge-equalisation circuits?
Each supercapacitor can only hold a low voltage (2–3 V), so many must be wired in series. Tiny differences in self-discharge cause some cells to hold more voltage than others. Charge-equalisation circuits monitor adjacent cells and move charge between them so no cell exceeds its safe voltage.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.73 • Batteries — How They Work
135What is a flywheel as an EV energy store?
A flywheel is a spinning disc that stores kinetic energy. The energy is released when the flywheel slows down. The flywheel can be reaccelerated by braking, acting as a very efficient regenerative brake, and can be coupled to wheels by a generator or a mechanical gearbox.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.73 • Batteries — How They Work
136Why is lead-acid still widely used despite its low specific energy?
Lead-acid is the cheapest rechargeable battery per kilowatt-hour stored and is built from cheap materials. It has a long track record, reliable performance and a wide range of efficient specific powers. For short-range vehicles like golf carts and wheelchairs, no better choice exists today.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.80 • Batteries — How They Work
137Why is lithium-ion now the preferred EV battery?
Lithium-ion batteries have specific energy around three times that of lead-acid, high energy density and good specific power, all in a smaller, lighter pack. Prices have dropped enough that big manufacturers now mass-produce EVs, like the Nissan Leaf, Mitsubishi MiEV, Tesla Roadster and Chevrolet Volt with Li-ion.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.69 • Batteries — How They Work
138Why does NiMH self-discharge faster than NiCad?
Hydrogen molecules are very small and can easily diffuse through the electrolyte to the positive electrode, where they react with the nickel oxyhydroxide. This slowly drains the cell. As a result, NiMH cells suffer noticeably faster self-discharge than the more stable NiCad cells.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.62 • Batteries — How They Work
139Why is the ZEBRA battery a poor fit for autonomous parked hybrids?
A ZEBRA battery needs to stay at about 320°C, which drains about 2.4 kWh (~13% of its stored energy) per day just for heating. So it must stay plugged in, which is impractical for a hybrid that may sit in an airport car park for two weeks.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.81 • Batteries — How They Work
140Why can't an aluminium-air battery power a road car on its own?
The aluminium-air battery has extremely low specific power: a 100 kg pack gives only about 1000 W. To get 20 kW you would need 2 tonnes of battery. So it is unsuitable as the sole power source for a road vehicle, though it could be used as a base load.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.70 • Batteries — How They Work
141Why is charge equalisation important for battery life?
Cells in a battery self-discharge at slightly different rates. Without equalisation, weaker cells fall lower with each cycle until one is fully flat. The pack then drives current backwards through it and ruins the cell. One damaged cell ruins the whole series battery.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.78 • Batteries — How They Work
142Why does the last bit of charging take much longer than the first?
Once most cells are nearly full, weaker cells still need to catch up. They can only be safely overcharged at a small current (about C/10). So the charger must keep trickling in current for hours to bring those laggard cells up to fully charged.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.78 • Batteries — How They Work
143Why are spinning flywheels considered dangerous?
A high-speed flywheel stores a lot of kinetic energy. If it ruptures in a crash, it releases that energy almost instantly, acting like a bomb. If it tears loose from its mountings it can also cause real damage. Plus, gyroscopic effects can affect the vehicle's handling.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.76 • Batteries — How They Work
144Why can't a flywheel store as much energy as we want?
Energy stored in a flywheel increases with its rotational speed squared. But as speed rises, internal stress in the disc also rises. Eventually the flywheel material's tensile strength sets a hard limit — beyond it the disc would burst. So energy storage is fundamentally capped.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.75 • Batteries — How They Work
145Why would you pair a zinc-air battery with a flywheel?
Zinc-air batteries can't be recharged on the vehicle, so they cannot directly accept regenerative braking energy. But pairing one with a flywheel lets the flywheel absorb the braking energy quickly and feed it back during acceleration, giving the vehicle a working regenerative-braking system.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.81 • Batteries — How They Work
146What is Peukert's law and what does the Peukert coefficient mean?
Peukert's law links battery capacity to discharge current with the formula Cp = (I^k)·T, where k is the Peukert coefficient. The lower k is, the less capacity falls when current rises. For lead-acid, k is typically about 1.2; other types tend to be lower.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.86 • Batteries — How They Work
147How is the internal resistance of a lead-acid battery estimated?
For a good-quality lead-acid traction battery, internal resistance is approximately 0.022 ohms per cell, scaled inversely by the amp-hour capacity. So for a 12 V (six-cell) battery rated 100 Ah at the 10-hour rate, R is roughly (0.022 × 6) ÷ 100 ohms.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.55 • Batteries — How They Work
148How can the Peukert coefficient be calculated from two ratings?
Take two capacity ratings at different discharge times — for example 42 Ah at 10 hours and 33.6 Ah at 1 hour. Convert to currents, then use k = (log T₁ − log T₂) ÷ (log I₂ − log I₁). The Peukert coefficient is the slope of log T versus log I.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.91 • Batteries — How They Work
149How would you roughly size a battery for a 180 km EV?
Estimate energy needed at the wheels (say 44 kWh for 180 km) and divide by ~70% motor and transmission efficiency, giving about 63 kWh. Then divide by the battery's specific energy. Lead-acid (35 Wh/kg) would weigh ~1257 kg; NiMH ~733 kg; zinc-air ~191 kg.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.97 • Batteries — How They Work
150Why is the projected 350 Wh/kg Li-ion specific energy by 2020 a big deal?
An Economist report cited by the textbook predicted lithium batteries could reach 350 Wh/kg by 2020 — about 2.5 times the cells in the Nissan Leaf (140 Wh/kg). At that point, EV range and energy density move within striking distance of internal combustion vehicles.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.97 • Batteries — How They Work
151How does grid electricity reach a home from a power station?
Electricity leaves the power station as AC, gets stepped up in voltage by a transformer, travels long distances along high-voltage transmission lines, then is stepped down again by another transformer near homes and businesses before reaching the customer's wall socket.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.97 • Charging & Electricity Supply
152What is the typical efficiency of grid electricity transmission?
Normal grid transmission delivers more than 90% of the power put in to the customer. Typical losses in the USA are about 6–8%. So roughly 6–8 units in every 100 are lost in wires, transformers and distribution between power station and your wall socket.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.97 • Charging & Electricity Supply
153Why are very high voltages used for long-distance transmission?
High-voltage lines, with voltages up to 150,000 V, cut transmission losses sharply. Losses can be kept as low as 3% per 1000 km. Higher voltages mean lower currents for the same power, and lower currents waste less energy as heat in the transmission wires.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.97 • Charging & Electricity Supply
154What is an electricity 'supergrid'?
A supergrid is a network of high-voltage transmission lines that lets electricity travel very long distances with low losses. Supergrids open up alternative energy options — for example, solar power generated in North Africa could be sent to Britain, or wind power moved between windy and calm regions.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.97 • Charging & Electricity Supply
155How much power does a normal home electrical socket supply?
Most domestic and workplace sockets in the developed world deliver up to about 3 kW of power. That is enough to slowly recharge an EV overnight or during a workday, but too slow for fast charging — those need much higher-power sockets and special cabling.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.97 • Charging & Electricity Supply
156How much power is needed to fast-charge a Nissan Leaf to 80%?
A Nissan Leaf has a 24 kWh battery. To recharge 80% of its capacity in about 48 minutes, a charging point must deliver around 24 kW of power. That is roughly eight times what a normal 3 kW home socket can supply.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.97 • Charging & Electricity Supply
157What infrastructure would a motorway service station need for EVs?
If 100 EVs an hour wanted to rapid-charge at a service station, each pulling about 24 kW, the site would need around 2.4 MW of installed capacity. So large-scale rapid charging requires careful planning of new grid connections, transformers and physical layout.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.98 • Charging & Electricity Supply
158How do electric trains pick up power from supply rails?
Electric trains take current from a third rail or overhead line through steel shoes or a pantograph that slides along as the train moves. The shoes are mounted on insulators so the rail or wire is electrically separate from the running track that carries the wheels.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.98 • Charging & Electricity Supply
159What is the voltage on the London Underground supply rails?
London Underground uses a four-rail DC supply. The rail next to the track runs at +420 V, while a central rail runs at +210 V. The current returns through the fourth rail rather than through the normal running rails carrying the train's wheels.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.98 • Charging & Electricity Supply
160What voltage does a French TGV high-speed train use?
The French TGV high-speed train uses an overhead supply line at 25,000 V AC, 50 Hz. The 700-Series Shinkansen in Japan uses a similar 25 kV system at 60 Hz. The pantograph on the train roof slides along the wire to pick up power as the train runs.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.98 • Charging & Electricity Supply
161Why are voltages above 1 kV usually only on overhead wires?
For safety, high voltages above about 1 kV are normally limited to overhead wiring well above ground rather than to a rail along the track that people might touch. Lower voltages such as 600 V or 750 V are common with third- or fourth-rail systems instead.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.98 • Charging & Electricity Supply
162What is inductive power transfer (IPT)?
Inductive power transfer is a wireless way to supply electrical energy to a vehicle. It uses the same principle as a transformer: an alternating current in a primary coil in the road induces current in a secondary pickup coil on the vehicle, with no mechanical contact needed.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.99 • Charging & Electricity Supply
163How does IPT cope with a large gap between road and vehicle?
A normal transformer needs a tiny gap between primary and secondary. IPT compensates for a much larger air gap (over 100 mm) by raising the operating frequency to about 15,000 Hz. The system then becomes insensitive to small positional shifts of the pickup over the road track.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.99 • Charging & Electricity Supply
164How efficient is IPT power transfer?
The track power supply and vehicle pickup of an IPT system can work with an efficiency of up to 96%. Both sides of the system are tuned (resonant), which minimises losses and unwanted harmonics. This makes IPT comparable in efficiency to a plug-in cable.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.100 • Charging & Electricity Supply
165What range of power can IPT systems transfer?
Inductive power transfer systems can transfer large amounts of power. The book lists power ranges from about 30 kW up to 1000 kW (1 MW), so IPT is capable of supplying not just cars but also buses, monorails and trams continuously along a track.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.100 • Charging & Electricity Supply
166Why is IPT considered low-maintenance and safe?
IPT has no brushes, sliding contacts or other moving parts, so it is virtually maintenance-free. All components are fully enclosed and insulated, making the system touch-proof. It also produces no carbon dust or sparks, so it is suitable for sensitive or hazardous environments.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.100 • Charging & Electricity Supply
167What is battery swapping?
Battery swapping is recharging an EV by physically removing its battery and replacing it with a fully charged one stored at the station. Spent batteries are then recharged in a warehouse for reuse. Denmark has a station that can swap a 250 kg lithium-ion pack in around five minutes.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.101 • Charging & Electricity Supply
168Why is battery swapping particularly suited to taxi fleets?
A taxi fleet has predictable usage patterns and a known number of vehicles, so battery swap logistics are simpler to manage. With battery swapping, an electric taxi can be kept running continuously throughout a 24-hour day, without long charging stops to wait through.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.101 • Charging & Electricity Supply
169What problems would mass battery swapping for cars need to solve?
Battery swapping for cars would need lots of space for storing and charging batteries, vehicles designed so packs can be removed quickly, and tight quality control so a swapped battery actually holds enough charge. Better future batteries would also reduce how often swaps are needed.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.102 • Charging & Electricity Supply
170What is the difference between AC and DC railway supply?
Railway supply can be either AC or DC. Lower voltages (600 V, 750 V) tend to use DC and third or fourth rails along the track. Voltages above 1 kV usually use overhead wires and may be AC (like the TGV's 25 kV, 50 Hz) or DC (like 1500 V, 3 kV).
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.98 • Charging & Electricity Supply
171Why does high-voltage DC transmission make sense only for long routes?
HVDC transmission needs expensive static inverters at each end to convert between AC and DC. These add capital cost and energy losses. Over long distances the savings in line losses outweigh the converter losses, but on short links those fixed costs aren't justified.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.97 • Charging & Electricity Supply
172Why would a supergrid help renewable energy in Europe?
Wind and solar power are intermittent and not always near demand. A supergrid lets electricity from sunny North Africa or a windy region be sent thousands of kilometres at low loss to cloudy Britain or calm Germany — balancing renewable supply across regions and seasons.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.97 • Charging & Electricity Supply
173Why might IPT be especially useful for taxis at a rank?
Inductive pickups can be buried in the road or in a station platform. A taxi waiting at a rank could top up its battery wirelessly without any cables or driver intervention. Lateral tolerance is generous, so the taxi doesn't need to be parked precisely.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.101 • Charging & Electricity Supply
174Why is standardisation of public chargers a problem?
Many EVs share public charging spots in car parks. If each car needs a different connector, voltage or protocol, drivers may not be able to safely connect to all units. The California Air Resources Board (CARB) has published guidelines aimed at standardising these car-to-charger connections.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.78 • Charging & Electricity Supply
175Why is most charging energy ultimately AC rather than DC?
Except for solar PV panels, almost all energy used to recharge a battery comes from an AC mains source. A charger must rectify and smooth this AC into DC before feeding the battery. Producing very smooth high-current DC is what makes large fast chargers expensive.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.76 • Charging & Electricity Supply
176Why must rectified DC be smoothed before feeding a battery?
Without smoothing, the rectified DC has ripple. At the dips the voltage falls below the battery's terminal voltage, so no charging happens, while at the peaks it can be high enough to damage the battery. Smoothing keeps voltage in the safe operating band continuously.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.76 • Charging & Electricity Supply
177Why is opportunistic charging risky if not done carefully?
Opportunistic charging adds small amounts of charge often. Over time, cells with slightly higher self-discharge fall behind and never get fully charged, eventually failing. So the textbook says users must occasionally give the battery a full, long charge to equalise all cells to 100%.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.79 • Charging & Electricity Supply
178Why is keeping a hybrid's battery partly empty actually desirable?
In a hybrid, the battery must always be able to absorb regenerative braking energy. If kept fully charged, it would have nowhere to put that energy. So the battery management system keeps it partly empty but periodically tops it up to 100% to equalise the cells.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.79 • Charging & Electricity Supply
179Why doesn't a hybrid car need a separate plug-in charger?
On a non-rechargeable hybrid, the alternator driven by the IC engine acts as the battery's charger. The vehicle's energy-management system controls when and how much charging happens. So the hybrid never needs a wall socket — it is refuelled at a petrol station instead.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.78 • Charging & Electricity Supply
180Why might IPT one day enable a 'pay-as-you-drive' road?
IPT lets vehicles draw power from buried road conductors with no contact. With electrified motorways using IPT, cars could charge while driving instead of stopping. The textbook suggests this could be applied to cars, buses and even hybrids, potentially revolutionising electric transport.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.101 • Charging & Electricity Supply
181Why is the European Commission backing HVDC links between countries?
In January 2009, the European Commission proposed €300 million to subsidise HVDC links between Ireland, Britain, the Netherlands, Germany, Denmark and Sweden. The aim is to integrate offshore wind farms and improve cross-border energy flows across Europe, supporting the rise of renewable power.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.97 • Charging & Electricity Supply
182Why is the Mediterranean Solar Plan interesting for EV electricity?
The Mediterranean Solar Plan aims to import large amounts of concentrating solar power into Europe from North Africa and the Middle East. If achieved, EVs charged on this supergrid would effectively be driving on solar energy, with the local emissions and well-to-wheel CO2 close to zero.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.97 • Charging & Electricity Supply
183Why can't existing home wiring usually do a fast EV charge?
Normal sockets supply about 3 kW. To recharge a 24 kWh Nissan Leaf to 80% in under an hour needs 24 kW — eight times more. That requires a dedicated higher-power circuit, thicker cabling, and often a new connection from the supply company at the home.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.97 • Charging & Electricity Supply
184Why might inductive charging suit car parks better than cables?
Inductive charging needs no plugging in by the driver — a coil under the parking bay does the work. There are no cables to trip on, vandalise or be left in the rain. The driver just parks, and the system handles power transfer automatically.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.98 • Charging & Electricity Supply
185Why is the IPT pickup designed with a special shape?
The pickup coil has a special shape that captures the magnetic field generated by the buried track conductors as efficiently as possible. The captured AC field induces electricity in the pickup, which is then converted to DC by the regulator on board the vehicle for use.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.99 • Charging & Electricity Supply
186Why is multi-load operation a useful IPT feature?
Using intelligent control, multiple vehicles can each be powered independently and at the same time on a single IPT track. So one buried cable along a road can serve any number of cars or buses simultaneously, with each one drawing only the power it needs.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.100 • Charging & Electricity Supply
187Why is IPT's unlimited operating speed important?
Mechanical pickup systems like conductor bars, festoons or cable reels have to keep contact, which limits how fast a vehicle can go before brushes spark or wear. IPT has no contact at all, so speed is not a limitation — the system works just as well at any vehicle speed.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.100 • Charging & Electricity Supply
188Why is the Wampfler test track at Weil am Rhein significant?
The Wampfler test track at Weil am Rhein, Germany, is claimed to be the largest IPT system built. It has a total capacity of 150 kW over a 400 m track length, with six pickups each rated at 25 kW and a 120 mm air gap.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.100 • Charging & Electricity Supply
189Why can excess power on the IPT test track be regenerated?
The test vehicle needs only about 10 kW peak, but six 25 kW pickups can supply much more. The system feeds the excess back through the conductor bar for regeneration into the mains. So energy isn't wasted — it returns to the grid, like an inverter on a solar panel.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.100 • Charging & Electricity Supply
