190Why does battery swapping get easier as batteries improve?
Higher specific energy and better efficiency mean each pack carries more useful range. So drivers come for swaps less often, the storage warehouse doesn't need to be as huge, and quality control is simpler. Better batteries shrink the logistical headache of running a swap network.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.102 • Charging & Electricity Supply
191Roughly how much grid capacity does mass EV fast charging need?
If 100 cars per hour each demand a 24 kW rapid charge at a single service area, that's a 2.4 MW load. Multiplied across thousands of service stations, that's gigawatts of new grid capacity, transformers and high-voltage lines — comparable to adding several new power stations.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.98 • Charging & Electricity Supply
192How does IPT achieve high efficiency despite a 100 mm air gap?
IPT runs at 15,000 Hz (vs. the 50/60 Hz of a normal transformer) and uses resonant tuning on both the primary and secondary side. The high frequency couples energy efficiently across the large air gap, while resonance minimises losses and harmonics, pushing efficiency up to 96%.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.100 • Charging & Electricity Supply
193Why is rectifier and smoothing cost a big factor in fast chargers?
The higher the DC charging current, the harder it is for rectifiers and smoothing circuits to deliver clean DC. So high-current chargers need expensive electronics. The General Motors EV1's charger, for example, cost about $2,000 in 1996 — a major slice of an early EV's bill.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.76 • Charging & Electricity Supply
194Why do many EVs use multiple-step charging for lead-acid?
Multiple-step charging raises the battery voltage to a preset upper level, then briefly removes the charge so the cell relaxes back to a lower preset level, then reapplies current. This avoids overheating and damage from sustained high voltage and is well supported by good commercial chargers.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.59 • Charging & Electricity Supply
195Why are pantograph systems used at high speeds instead of third rails?
At high speeds, contact rails along the ground are impractical and unsafe due to the high voltages used. Pantographs slide along an overhead wire, where high voltages (e.g. 25 kV on the TGV and Shinkansen) can be safely kept far above passengers and lineside workers.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.98 • Charging & Electricity Supply
196How does an IPT system include data alongside power?
An IPT system can pass signals and data along the same path it uses for power, with minimal additional hardware. An integrated power and data system is in development, meaning a single in-road infrastructure could provide both energy and communication links to passing vehicles.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.100 • Charging & Electricity Supply
197Why does the textbook expect future EV charging to mix several approaches?
No single method fits every case. Overnight charging at home covers most days. Workplace and public car park chargers handle daytime top-ups. Rapid 24+ kW chargers help on motorways. Inductive charging suits parking bays, and battery swapping works for fleets like taxis.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.97 • Charging & Electricity Supply
198What does 'long-track' capability of IPT make possible?
IPT works along track lengths of up to several kilometres, and these long tracks can be repeated to give even longer systems. That means a buried IPT cable could electrify a whole bus route, motorway lane or industrial loop — vehicles charge continuously as they drive.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.100 • Charging & Electricity Supply
199Why might IPT be safer than overhead wires for theme park rides?
IPT has fully enclosed and insulated components, no carbon dust, no sparks and no exposed wires. That makes it ideal for sensitive environments like theme parks and indoor monorails where overhead wires would be ugly, dangerous and hard to maintain near the public.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.100 • Charging & Electricity Supply
200Why does the textbook compare battery swap warehouses to fuel depots?
Both store energy for vehicles. A petrol depot keeps fuel; a battery swap warehouse keeps charged packs. As future batteries get more energy-dense and recharge less often, swap warehouses get smaller — much like fuel-station tanks shrink if engines get more efficient.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.102 • Charging & Electricity Supply
201What is a fuel cell?
A fuel cell is a battery-like device that uses hydrogen and oxygen to produce electricity directly through a chemical reaction. The basic reaction combines hydrogen with oxygen to form water and release energy as electric current, with no burning involved at the cell.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.102 • Fuel Cells & Hydrogen
202Why is a fuel cell vehicle called 'zero-emission'?
The types of fuel cells used in vehicles work at about 85°C, low enough that no nitrous oxides form from air. The only output is water and electricity, so the car produces no harmful exhaust. That is why a fuel-cell vehicle can be described as zero-emission.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.102 • Fuel Cells & Hydrogen
203When was the hydrogen fuel cell invented?
The hydrogen fuel cell was invented around 1840. So the basic technology is nearly two centuries old, yet fuel cells have still not become a mainstream power source for vehicles. They are now expected to gradually make their mark over the next 20 to 30 years.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.102 • Fuel Cells & Hydrogen
204What is the most important type of fuel cell for EVs?
For EVs, the most important type is the PEM (proton-exchange-membrane) fuel cell, also called PEMFC. Its electrolyte is a solid polymer in which protons can move. The PEMFC is used in all the most impressive demonstration fuel-cell vehicles built so far.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.107 • Fuel Cells & Hydrogen
205What happens at the anode of a PEM fuel cell?
At the anode of a PEM fuel cell, hydrogen gas is split. Each H₂ molecule loses two electrons and becomes two H⁺ ions (protons). The electrons flow out through the external circuit as electric current, while the H⁺ ions travel through the polymer electrolyte to the cathode.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.106 • Fuel Cells & Hydrogen
206What happens at the cathode of a PEM fuel cell?
At the cathode, oxygen molecules from the air combine with electrons coming back through the external circuit and with H⁺ ions arriving through the electrolyte. They form water, the only product of the cell. This is what makes fuel-cell vehicles so clean.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.106 • Fuel Cells & Hydrogen
207What does PEM stand for in PEM fuel cell?
PEM stands for proton-exchange membrane. The membrane is a solid polymer with mobile H⁺ ions (protons) baked into it, so it carries current as positive ions while blocking electrons. The electrons must instead travel through the external circuit, producing useful electric power.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.106 • Fuel Cells & Hydrogen
208Why does a PEM fuel cell use platinum?
PEM fuel cells run at low temperatures (about 80°C), so the reactions are naturally slow. Platinum is used as a catalyst to speed them up. Thanks to development, only minute amounts of platinum are now needed, and its cost is a small part of the cell's total price.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.107 • Fuel Cells & Hydrogen
209What is a direct methanol fuel cell (DMFC)?
A direct methanol fuel cell is a PEM fuel cell that uses liquid methanol directly as fuel, without first reforming it into hydrogen. This makes refuelling easy, but DMFCs have very low power. Their main near-term use is in portable electronics, not vehicles.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.107 • Fuel Cells & Hydrogen
210What is an alkaline fuel cell (AFC) historically known for?
The alkaline fuel cell powered NASA's Apollo missions and Shuttle Orbiter. It can be cheaper than a PEMFC but has lower power, and its alkaline electrolyte reacts with carbon dioxide from air, making terrestrial use difficult. A few demonstration EVs have used hybrid AFC/battery systems.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.108 • Fuel Cells & Hydrogen
211What temperature does a solid oxide fuel cell (SOFC) operate at?
Solid oxide fuel cells run in the region of 600 to 1000°C. The high temperature allows good reaction rates without expensive catalysts and even allows the cell to use gases such as natural gas directly. However, the ceramic materials and start-up requirements make SOFCs unsuitable for road cars.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.108 • Fuel Cells & Hydrogen
212Why does a fuel cell electrode have a porous structure?
Fuel cell reactions take place where gas, electrolyte ions and electrons meet on the electrode surface. The larger the surface area, the more current can flow. Electrodes are made highly porous so their real surface area is much greater than their external length × width.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.109 • Fuel Cells & Hydrogen
213How thin is a PEM fuel cell's membrane electrode assembly (MEA)?
Everything in a PEM fuel cell is very thin. The electrolyte itself is about 0.05–0.1 mm thick, each electrode is about 0.03 mm thick, and gas diffusion layers are 0.2–0.5 mm thick each. The whole anode/electrolyte/cathode (MEA) sandwich is around 1 mm thick.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.111 • Fuel Cells & Hydrogen
214Why isn't a fuel cell limited by Carnot's law?
Internal-combustion engines are heat engines, so their efficiency is capped by the Carnot limit. Fuel cells convert chemical energy directly into electricity without going through a heat-engine cycle. As a result, they aren't constrained by Carnot and can in theory be more efficient than IC engines.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.111 • Fuel Cells & Hydrogen
215What is the typical operating voltage of a working PEM fuel cell?
A working PEM fuel cell typically operates at an average cell voltage of about 0.65–0.70 V, even when drawing currents close to 1 A per cm² of electrode area. This corresponds to an electrical efficiency (against the HHV) of about 50% — much higher than any IC engine.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.117 • Fuel Cells & Hydrogen
216Why is a fuel cell more efficient at low loads?
A fuel cell's voltage is higher at lower current. Since electrical energy is voltage × charge, higher voltage means higher efficiency. So a fuel cell is more efficient at part-load — the opposite of an IC engine, which is particularly inefficient at low power.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.117 • Fuel Cells & Hydrogen
217What is a fuel cell 'stack'?
A stack is a number of fuel cells connected in series to produce a useful total voltage. Since a single cell only gives about 0.7 V while running, many cells (sometimes 80 or more) are stacked together to drive a motor or other load. Stacks look like solid blocks.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.119 • Fuel Cells & Hydrogen
218What is a bipolar plate?
A bipolar plate sits between two cells in a fuel cell stack. It makes electrical contact across the whole face of the cathode of one cell and the anode of the next cell at the same time, and also feeds oxygen and hydrogen to each side without letting them mix.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.119 • Fuel Cells & Hydrogen
219Why are fuel cells expensive to make?
Each stack has many bipolar plates that are complex to manufacture. Graphite is hard to machine, stainless steel can corrode, and plastics don't conduct well. Add seals, gas diffusion layers, catalyst-coated electrodes and assembly with very low fault tolerance — and unit cost stays high.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.120 • Fuel Cells & Hydrogen
220Why is water management critical in a PEM fuel cell?
PEM fuel cells produce water at the cathode. The polymer electrolyte must stay well hydrated to conduct H⁺ ions, but if too much water builds up the cell floods. So engineers must keep the membrane wet enough to work, without drowning the air-side reactions.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.122 • Fuel Cells & Hydrogen
221What is Nafion?
Nafion (made by Dupont) is the best-known proton exchange membrane material. It is a fluoropolymer related to PTFE (Teflon) but with sulfonated side chains. These attract water, creating hydrated regions through which H⁺ ions can move easily. It is the industry-standard PEMFC electrolyte.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.123 • Fuel Cells & Hydrogen
222Why is hydrogen storage so hard?
Hydrogen has very high specific energy (per kilogram) but very low density. So getting useful amounts into a small space needs very high pressures. It is also hard to liquefy — even at 22 K, liquid hydrogen has a density of only about 71 kg/m³.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.141 • Fuel Cells & Hydrogen
223What are the main ways to store hydrogen?
Hydrogen can be stored as a compressed gas in cylinders, as a cryogenic liquid (LH₂) at about 22 K, absorbed in a reversible metal hydride, or in carbon nanofibres. Chemical hydrogen carriers like methanol or sodium borohydride also act as an indirect storage method.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.141 • Fuel Cells & Hydrogen
224Is hydrogen more dangerous than other fuels?
Hydrogen needs careful handling — it leaks easily and is highly flammable. But all things considered, it is no more dangerous, and in some respects less dangerous, than common fuels like methane and propane, provided systems are well designed and regularly checked for leaks.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.141 • Fuel Cells & Hydrogen
225How is hydrogen stored as compressed gas in a vehicle?
Hydrogen on vehicles is typically stored in cylinders with an aluminium inner liner wrapped in an aramide-fibre/epoxy composite, rated for about 300 bar working pressure and around 1200 bar burst pressure. The composite tears rather than shattering, giving good burst behaviour for safety.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.141 • Fuel Cells & Hydrogen
226At what temperature is liquid hydrogen (LH₂) stored?
Liquid hydrogen, abbreviated LH₂, is stored at about 22 Kelvin (around minus 251°C). At that temperature its density is still only about 71 kg per cubic metre. The container is a large, strongly reinforced vacuum (Dewar) flask kept below 3 bar of pressure.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.143 • Fuel Cells & Hydrogen
227How does a reversible metal hydride store hydrogen?
Certain metal alloys (with titanium, iron, manganese, nickel, etc.) react with hydrogen at low pressure to form a metal hydride, soaking up the gas. Heating the hydride later releases the hydrogen on demand. The reaction is fully reversible — the alloy acts like a hydrogen sponge.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.145 • Fuel Cells & Hydrogen
228What is electrolysis of water?
Electrolysis uses electricity to split water into hydrogen and oxygen. Two inert electrodes are placed in water; hydrogen forms at the negative electrode and oxygen at the positive. If the electricity comes from nuclear or renewable sources, this produces carbon-free hydrogen.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.141 • Fuel Cells & Hydrogen
229What is fuel reforming?
Fuel reforming is making hydrogen from another fuel like methanol, methane or petrol. Steam reforming and partial oxidation both extract hydrogen, often using a catalyst. The textbook notes that the thermodynamic efficiency of reforming is between about 70% and 85% (LHV basis).
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.141 • Fuel Cells & Hydrogen
230Why is methanol a popular hydrogen carrier?
Methanol can be reformed to hydrogen at around 250°C — much lower than petrol, with far less carbon monoxide produced. It is liquid, easy to handle, and yields about 0.188 kg of hydrogen per kg of methanol via steam reforming. Many automakers have worked on methanol fuel-cell vehicles.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.149 • Fuel Cells & Hydrogen
231What is sodium borohydride and how is it used?
Sodium borohydride (NaBH₄) is a chemical hydrogen carrier. A solution reacts with water in the presence of a catalyst to release pure hydrogen and form sodium borate. Removing the catalyst stops the reaction, so hydrogen generation is highly controllable — useful for fuel-cell vehicles.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.152 • Fuel Cells & Hydrogen
232What is the Necar 5?
The Necar 5, launched by DaimlerChrysler in November 2000, represents the state of the art in methanol fuel-cell vehicles. Based on the A-Class Mercedes, it uses a Ballard 75 kW stack with an onboard methanol reformer under the passenger compartment and reaches over 150 kph.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.150 • Fuel Cells & Hydrogen
233What are PEMFC bipolar plate materials made from?
Bipolar plates are typically made of graphite or stainless steel. Graphite is difficult to machine and brittle. Stainless steel can corrode in some fuel-cell types. Plastics would be easy to mould but don't conduct electricity well, so engineers are still searching for a better material.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.120 • Fuel Cells & Hydrogen
234Why is hydrogen leak rate higher than other gases?
Hydrogen molecules are very small and very light. They diffuse through tiny gaps faster than any other gas — about 2.8 times faster than methane and 3.3 times faster than air. So fuel-cell systems must be designed with the lowest possible chance of leaks.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.141 • Fuel Cells & Hydrogen
235What are carbon nanofibres in hydrogen storage?
Carbon nanofibres are tiny tube-like carbon structures that can absorb hydrogen. A 1998 paper claimed they could store over 67% hydrogen by mass. That number has not been reproduced, but other groups have shown promising capacity, making nanofibres one to watch for the future.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.147 • Fuel Cells & Hydrogen
236Why are fuel-cell buses easier than fuel-cell cars to deploy first?
Buses refuel at one depot, so building hydrogen filling infrastructure is far simpler than for cars that fill up anywhere. Buses also run all day in cities where local emissions matter most. Many fuel-cell buses are already in service in Canada, the USA and Europe.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.106 • Fuel Cells & Hydrogen
237Why might a hydrogen IC engine still compete with a fuel cell?
A hydrogen IC engine emits only a little nitrous oxide — no CO, CO₂ or hydrocarbons. It is cheaper and uses proven technology. While fuel cells can be more efficient, the cost gap, plus a hydrogen IC engine in a hybrid drivetrain, may still tip the balance. Time will tell.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.117 • Fuel Cells & Hydrogen
238Why is the SOFC unsuitable for cars even though it's efficient?
Solid oxide fuel cells operate at 600–1000°C. The ceramic materials are difficult to handle, expensive to make, and need air pre-heaters and special cooling. They also start up slowly. So they suit always-on power plants better than cars, which start and stop many times a day.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.108 • Fuel Cells & Hydrogen
239Why must internal manifolding be used in PEM fuel cells?
Because the electrodes are porous, gas can escape from their edges. So edges are sealed with gaskets and the bipolar plates carry gas to the electrode surface through internal channels and small holes — this is internal manifolding. The result looks like a sealed solid block.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.120 • Fuel Cells & Hydrogen
240Why is LH₂ inherently inefficient to produce?
Liquefying hydrogen takes several stages: compress, cool to about 78 K with liquid nitrogen, then expand through a turbine, plus a special isomer-conversion step. Even in the best plants, about 25% of the hydrogen's heating value is consumed. Smaller plants consume up to 45%.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.144 • Fuel Cells & Hydrogen
241Why do metal hydride stores need high-purity hydrogen?
Impurities react permanently with the metal alloy, contaminating the absorber and reducing its capacity. So the hydrogen used must be at least 99.999% pure. That is one of the main drawbacks of metal hydride storage, alongside heavy weight and slow filling.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.147 • Fuel Cells & Hydrogen
242Why are metal hydride stores actually quite safe?
The hydrogen in a metal hydride is held at modest pressure (a few bar), not the hundreds of bar of a compressed cylinder. If the container is damaged, gas cools as it releases, slowing further release. So there is no sudden, dangerous discharge — a key safety advantage.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.147 • Fuel Cells & Hydrogen
243Why is methanol reforming cleaner than petrol reforming?
Methanol reforming happens at around 250°C, far below the temperatures needed for petrol. It also produces much less carbon monoxide, so much less clean-up is required to feed the resulting hydrogen to a PEM fuel cell. Methanol is also a liquid, simple to handle.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.149 • Fuel Cells & Hydrogen
244Why is sodium borohydride limited despite its safety advantages?
Sodium borohydride is the safest liquid hydrogen carrier, gives pure hydrogen and is easy to control. But it is very expensive — about $630 per kg of hydrogen produced, more than 100× the cost of using grid electricity to electrolyse water. Production cost must fall first.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.155 • Fuel Cells & Hydrogen
245Why is fuel cell electrode area such a vital figure of merit?
Gas, ions and electrons all have to meet on the electrode surface for current to flow. The larger the electrode area, the more of these reactions can happen per second. So fuel-cell performance is usually quoted in current per square centimetre, not just total power.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.109 • Fuel Cells & Hydrogen
246What three losses cause a fuel cell's voltage to drop with current?
Three losses act in series: activation loss (energy needed to start the cathode reaction, causes initial drop), ohmic loss (resistance of electrolyte and electrodes, follows Ohm's law), and concentration loss (oxygen runs short at high currents, voltage drops fast). Together they shape the cell's V-I curve.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.117 • Fuel Cells & Hydrogen
247How is fuel cell efficiency calculated from its voltage?
If a fuel cell were 100% efficient (using HHV), each cell would produce 1.48 V. Real cells output a lower voltage V_c. Efficiency equals V_c divided by 1.48 V (or 1.25 V if using LHV). So measuring stack voltage and dividing by cell count gives instant efficiency.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.115 • Fuel Cells & Hydrogen
248Why is the Nernst voltage rise smaller than the real voltage rise with pressure?
The Nernst equation predicts a small voltage rise when pressure doubles. But in real low-temperature PEM cells the actual rise is nearly ten times bigger, because pressure also reduces the cathode activation voltage loss. Even so, the energy cost of pumping the air up often outweighs the gain.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.119 • Fuel Cells & Hydrogen
249Why do PEMFC vehicles still run their cells above atmospheric pressure?
PEM fuel cell stacks in vehicles run at 1.5–2.0 bar, slightly above ambient. The aim isn't mainly the small voltage boost. It is that pressurising the air makes water balance in the PEM electrolyte much easier to manage — keeping the membrane wet enough without flooding the cathode.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.119 • Fuel Cells & Hydrogen
250How does Nafion's sulfonated side chain enable proton conduction?
Nafion's polymer backbone is hydrophobic (water-hating), but each sulfonated side chain ends in HSO₃, which is hydrophilic. Water clusters around these acid groups, creating dilute-acid channels through which H⁺ ions can hop. Around 20 water molecules per side chain gives a conductivity of about 0.1 S/cm.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.125 • Fuel Cells & Hydrogen
251What is a brushed DC motor?
A brushed DC motor is the simplest electric motor type. It has permanent magnets (or coils) producing the magnetic field, a rotating armature carrying current via brushes and a commutator that reverses the current at the right moment to keep the rotor turning in one direction.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.162 • Electric Motors & Controllers
252What does the commutator do in a brushed DC motor?
The commutator is a split-ring contact on the rotor. As the rotor turns, the commutator reverses the direction of current in the armature coil at exactly the right moment. This keeps the force on the coil acting in the same direction, so the rotor keeps turning.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.162 • Electric Motors & Controllers
253What is the armature in a DC motor?
The armature is the rotating wire coil of a DC motor, wound around an iron core to strengthen the magnetic field. As current flows through it, the magnetic field exerts a force that turns the rotor and produces useful torque on the motor's output shaft.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.162 • Electric Motors & Controllers
254What is back EMF in a DC motor?
As the motor armature turns through the magnetic field, it acts as a generator and produces its own voltage. This voltage opposes the supply voltage and is called the back EMF. The faster the motor spins, the larger the back EMF, which reduces the current flowing in.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.164 • Electric Motors & Controllers
255Why does a DC motor have maximum torque at zero speed?
At zero speed there is no back EMF, so the full supply voltage drives current through the armature resistance, giving the largest possible current and hence the largest torque. As the motor speeds up, back EMF grows, current drops and torque steadily falls with increasing rotational speed.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.166 • Electric Motors & Controllers
256How do you control the speed of a brushed DC motor?
You can control a brushed DC motor's speed in two ways: by varying the supply voltage to the armature, or, where the magnetic field is produced by coils, by varying the field current. Lowering the supply voltage reduces maximum torque, while weakening the field gives high-speed/low-torque operation.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.167 • Electric Motors & Controllers
257What is a separately excited DC motor?
In a separately excited DC motor, the field winding (which sets the magnetic field) is fed from a separate supply, independent of the armature. So the field current and armature voltage can be controlled separately, giving great flexibility in choosing torque and speed over a wide range.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.169 • Electric Motors & Controllers
258What are the four main losses in an electric motor?
An electric motor has four main losses: copper losses (I²R heating in the windings and brushes), iron losses (hysteresis and eddy currents in the iron core), friction-and-windage losses, and a constant loss for things like field windings or control electronics that run all the time.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.169 • Electric Motors & Controllers
259What are copper losses in a motor?
Copper losses are the I²R heating losses caused by the electrical resistance of the motor's windings and brushes. They depend on the square of the current, which is itself proportional to torque. In small motors, copper losses are usually the largest source of inefficiency.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.169 • Electric Motors & Controllers
260What are iron losses?
Iron losses come from the iron parts of the rotor experiencing a constantly changing magnetic field. Two types occur: hysteresis (energy needed to repeatedly re-magnetise the iron) and eddy currents (small circulating currents induced in the iron). Both losses grow with speed and waste energy as heat.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.169 • Electric Motors & Controllers
261Why are motor rotors made from thin laminated sheets?
Eddy currents flow in loops within the iron rotor, heating it and wasting energy. Building the rotor from many thin iron sheets, each separated by a layer of paint, breaks up these loops by raising the iron's effective electrical resistance, greatly reducing eddy-current losses.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.169 • Electric Motors & Controllers
262Why can a motor be driven over its rated power for short periods?
A motor's rated power is the level it can run at all day without overheating. Because the motor takes time to warm up, it can briefly handle higher power if it's not already hot. EVs exploit this during short bursts of acceleration when extra power is needed.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.172 • Electric Motors & Controllers
263What is dynamic braking?
Dynamic braking slows a motor by switching it from the battery to a load resistor. The motor's kinetic energy is converted back to electricity, which is then dissipated as heat in the resistor. You control braking torque by choosing the resistor value — no energy is recovered, though.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.173 • Electric Motors & Controllers
264What is regenerative braking electrically?
In regenerative braking, the motor is connected to a battery or capacitor instead of a resistor. Kinetic energy is converted to electricity by the motor and stored in the battery for reuse, slowing the vehicle. A DC/DC converter is normally needed to match motor voltage to battery voltage.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.173 • Electric Motors & Controllers
265What is a MOSFET and where is it used in EV electronics?
A MOSFET (metal-oxide-semiconductor field-effect transistor) is an electronic switch turned on by a small gate voltage. It has a very low on-state resistance (sometimes 0.01 Ω) for low-voltage devices and is widely used in EV power electronic systems below about 1 kW.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.176 • Electric Motors & Controllers
266What is an IGBT?
An IGBT (insulated gate bipolar transistor) combines a bipolar transistor with a MOSFET, taking advantages of both. It needs only a low gate voltage to switch and handles higher voltages and currents than MOSFETs. IGBTs are now the universal switch from about 1 kW up to hundreds of kilowatts.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.176 • Electric Motors & Controllers
267What is a step-down (buck) converter?
A step-down or buck converter is a switching circuit that produces a lower output voltage from a higher input voltage. Using an electronic switch, an inductor and a diode, it can chop the input on and off so the average output voltage is whatever you choose.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.177 • Electric Motors & Controllers
268What efficiency can a step-down chopper achieve?
A typical step-down chopper circuit should reach more than 90% efficiency. In higher-voltage systems of around 100 V or more, even higher efficiencies — as much as 98% — are possible. This makes the buck regulator a very efficient way to control motor voltage.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.178 • Electric Motors & Controllers
269What is a step-up (boost) converter?
A step-up or boost converter raises a lower DC voltage to a higher one. It stores energy briefly in an inductor while the switch is on, then releases it through a diode to charge a capacitor at a higher voltage. Essential for regenerative braking into a battery.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.178 • Electric Motors & Controllers
270What is an inverter?
An inverter is a circuit that converts DC from a battery or fuel cell into AC needed by motors like the induction motor or brushless DC motor. It uses four or six electronic switches in an H-bridge or three-phase configuration to chop the DC into alternating waveforms.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.181 • Electric Motors & Controllers
271What is pulse-width modulation (PWM)?
Pulse-width modulation is a technique where an inverter switches its electronic switches on and off many times per AC cycle, varying the on-time of each pulse. The result is an output current that more closely resembles a sine wave, with weaker harmonics that cause less motor heating.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.183 • Electric Motors & Controllers
272What is a three-phase AC supply?
A three-phase supply has three alternating voltages, equal in size but 120° out of phase. Most large EV motors use three coils fed by three-phase AC. A three-phase inverter uses six electronic switches with freewheeling diodes to generate the three phases from a DC source.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.183 • Electric Motors & Controllers
273What is a brushless DC (BLDC) motor?
A BLDC motor is really an AC motor that takes its alternating current from an inverter fed by a DC supply. Its rotor is a permanent magnet, and electronics switch the stator coils to keep the rotor turning. There are no brushes — they're replaced by electronic commutation.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.185 • Electric Motors & Controllers
274How does a BLDC motor know when to switch its coils?
The current switching must be synchronised with rotor position. BLDC motors use rotor-position sensors — usually Hall-effect sensors that detect the rotor magnets, or optical sensors. The controller microprocessor reads these signals and switches the coil currents at exactly the right moment.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.186 • Electric Motors & Controllers
275What is a switched reluctance motor (SRM)?
An SRM has a simple iron rotor with no magnets or coils. The stator carries coils. When a coil is energised, the rotor is pulled into alignment with the magnetic field, minimising the air gap. Switching coils in sequence turns the rotor smoothly with simple, rugged construction.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.189 • Electric Motors & Controllers
276Why is the SRM rotor so simple?
In an SRM the rotor is just a piece of magnetically soft iron with shaped 'salient poles' — there are no windings, brushes or permanent magnets on it. The rotor is therefore simple, cheap, rugged and can run at high speeds without flux from rotor magnets limiting the design.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.190 • Electric Motors & Controllers
277What is an induction motor?
An induction motor has three-phase coils on the stator producing a rotating magnetic field, and a 'cage' of copper or aluminium bars as the rotor. The rotating field induces currents in the bars, which then experience a force that drags the rotor along after the field.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.193 • Electric Motors & Controllers
278What is 'slip' in an induction motor?
Slip is the difference between the speed of the stator's rotating magnetic field and the speed of the rotor. The rotor must run slightly slower than the field, so that field lines keep cutting the rotor bars and inducing current. If they matched, no torque would be produced.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.193 • Electric Motors & Controllers
279How is induction motor speed controlled in an EV?
Induction motor speed is controlled by varying the frequency of the three-phase supply from the inverter. A higher frequency means a faster rotating magnetic field and a faster rotor. Speed feedback adjusts frequency to keep the actual speed close to the desired value despite slip variations.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.195 • Electric Motors & Controllers
280Why are induction motors so widely used despite being slightly less efficient?
Induction motors are produced in very high volumes, making them reasonably priced. Their technology is highly mature, very reliable, and uses cheap, well-developed materials. The 1–2% efficiency drop versus brushless types is offset by ruggedness, simple rotor (no magnets) and proven long service life.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.195 • Electric Motors & Controllers
281What is an efficiency map for a motor?
An efficiency map is a chart that plots motor efficiency at every combination of torque and speed. It shows where the motor performs best — typically a narrow sweet spot — and where efficiency drops. Engineers use it to choose a motor matched to the vehicle's typical operating conditions.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.172 • Electric Motors & Controllers
282Why do larger motors tend to be more efficient?
Larger motors have better surface-area-to-volume ratios and can dissipate heat more easily. Their relative copper, iron and friction losses are smaller as a fraction of total power. Class-1 efficiency standards bear this out for induction motors, and the same trend applies to BLDC and SRM types.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.195 • Electric Motors & Controllers
283Why are higher-speed motors usually more efficient?
Many motor losses are proportional to torque, not power. For the same power, a higher-speed motor produces less torque and so has lower copper losses. That's why EV drivetrains often pair a high-speed motor with a 10:1 reduction gearbox rather than a direct-drive low-speed motor.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.195 • Electric Motors & Controllers
284Why does motor mass depend more on torque than power?
Motor size is set mostly by the torque it must produce, not its peak power. So a lighter, smaller motor can deliver the same power at higher speed and lower torque. EVs typically use a high-speed motor with a gearbox to save weight and space versus a slow direct-drive motor.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.198 • Electric Motors & Controllers
285What is an 'inside-out' motor in hybrid vehicles?
An inside-out motor reverses the layout: the stationary coils sit on the inside, while the rotor — a band of permanent magnets — turns on the outside. It's a flat, large-diameter design that fits on the engine crankshaft. The centrifugal force keeps the magnets held against the rotor.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.199 • Electric Motors & Controllers
286Why is the brushed DC motor at a fundamental size disadvantage?
In a brushed DC motor virtually all losses happen in the rotor at the centre, where heat is hard to remove. In brushless designs most losses happen on the outer stator, which can be cooled by air or liquid easily, so brushless motors can be smaller.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.172 • Electric Motors & Controllers
287Why does a DC/DC converter make regenerative braking possible?
When braking, the motor often generates a voltage lower than the battery's. Without a converter, no current would flow into the battery, so no energy would be recovered. A boost DC/DC converter raises the motor's low voltage to the battery's higher voltage, letting energy flow back in.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.174 • Electric Motors & Controllers
288Why are linear voltage regulators banned in EV traction?
A linear regulator drops voltage by turning surplus electrical power into heat. In a battery EV, that wastes precious energy. Linear regulators have no place in systems where efficiency is paramount, which is why switching choppers (buck/boost) are used to control motor voltage in electric vehicles.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.178 • Electric Motors & Controllers
289Why does PWM produce less motor heating than simple square-wave inverters?
Simple square-wave AC contains many high-frequency harmonics, which speed up iron losses in the motor. PWM uses many short pulses per cycle, giving a current closer to a sine wave with weaker harmonics. Less harmonic content means less iron loss and less heating in the motor.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.183 • Electric Motors & Controllers
290Why is the BLDC motor sometimes called a permanent-magnet synchronous motor?
Its rotor is a permanent magnet, and the inverter feeds AC into the stator coils. The rotor turns in synchronism with the supply frequency, just like a synchronous AC motor. That's why the BLDC is also called a self-synchronous AC motor, variable-frequency synchronous motor or electronically commutated motor.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.185 • Electric Motors & Controllers
291Why are SRMs sometimes called noisy compared to other motors?
Each SRM coil is pulsed on and off at exactly the right moment. Small errors in timing cause torque ripple, producing a 'thumping' acoustic noise. With more coils and digital signal processors to do precise timing, modern SRMs are much smoother and quieter than early designs.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.191 • Electric Motors & Controllers
292Why are SRMs especially good across a wide range of speed and torque?
Although peak efficiency of an SRM may be a little below the BLDC motor's, SRMs hold their efficiency over a much wider range of speed and torque than other motor types. That makes them well suited to vehicles, which spend a lot of time away from any single 'sweet spot'.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.192 • Electric Motors & Controllers
293Why are PM synchronous motors replacing induction motors in many EVs?
Permanent-magnet synchronous motors have a higher torque-per-litre ratio than induction motors, and falling magnet manufacturing costs make them economical. So they tend to give more torque from less volume and weight than induction motors, which is exactly what EV designers want to fit under the hood.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.189 • Electric Motors & Controllers
294Why might an EV use a high-speed motor with a gearbox instead of direct drive?
Motor size scales with torque. A high-speed motor with a 10:1 gearbox gives the wheel torque you need from a small, light motor. A direct-drive low-speed motor would need to be much bigger and heavier. So gearing trades a small reduction in efficiency for a major saving in motor mass.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.198 • Electric Motors & Controllers
295Why is liquid cooling worth the complexity in high-power EV motors?
Motor power per kilogram is set by how fast you can carry away the waste heat. Liquid cooling lifts heat out of the motor much faster than air, so the same motor frame can handle more power. It also runs cooler, lowering winding resistance and improving efficiency by about 1%.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.197 • Electric Motors & Controllers
296What formula gives total motor losses combining all four loss types?
The textbook combines copper losses (kc·T²), iron+friction losses proportional to angular speed (kᵢ·ω plus windage), and a constant offset C. Total losses ≈ kc·T² + kᵢ·ω + kw·ω³ + C. Plugging this and output power into η = P_out/(P_out + losses) gives the motor's efficiency.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.169 • Electric Motors & Controllers
297Why does a brushed DC motor's stall torque calculation produce a huge number?
At zero speed there is no back EMF, so only the small armature resistance limits current. A 24 V supply across 0.016 Ω could in theory drive 1500 A and 205 N·m of torque. A current limit (around 250–350 A for 5 s) is essential to protect the motor.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.167 • Electric Motors & Controllers
298Why is the boost regulator's efficiency a bit lower than the buck's?
In a boost converter, the inductor and switch currents are higher than the output current, and all charge passes through the diode (0.6 V drop), adding losses. So efficiency is usually a bit lower than for a buck regulator. Still, over 80% is normal — and 95%+ above 100 V.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.180 • Electric Motors & Controllers
299How does a six-pole motor differ from a two-pole induction motor?
An induction motor's magnetic field can be wound to have two, four, six or more poles. The field rotates at the supply frequency divided by the number of pole pairs. So a six-pole motor turns one-third the speed of a two-pole on the same AC supply.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.193 • Electric Motors & Controllers
300Why does an inverter limit induction motor torque at high speed?
Coil impedance rises with frequency, so at higher frequencies the same voltage drives less current. With a fixed-voltage source (battery or fuel cell) and a frequency-controlled inverter, available current — and so available torque — falls as speed rises. Maximum torque ends up roughly inversely proportional to speed.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.195 • Electric Motors & Controllers
301What is 'tractive effort' in vehicle modelling?
Tractive effort is the forward force the vehicle's powertrain delivers to the road through its drive wheels. It must do four jobs at once: overcome rolling resistance, push through aerodynamic drag, climb any slope and, if speed is changing, accelerate or slow down the vehicle.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.204 • How an EV Performs — Modelling Basics
302What is rolling resistance?
Rolling resistance is the force opposing motion mainly due to hysteresis losses in the tyres, plus friction in bearings and gears. It is roughly constant with speed and is proportional to vehicle weight. Hard, well-inflated tyres have low rolling resistance and let the vehicle roll easily.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.204 • How an EV Performs — Modelling Basics
303What is the coefficient of rolling resistance μ_rr?
μ_rr is the dimensionless number that links rolling resistance force to vehicle weight: force equals μ_rr × weight. A typical radial-ply tyre has μ_rr around 0.015. Special low-resistance tyres developed for EVs can get the value down to about 0.005.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.204 • How an EV Performs — Modelling Basics
304What is aerodynamic drag?
Aerodynamic drag is the force the body of the moving vehicle must push against the air. It is proportional to air density, the vehicle's frontal area, the drag coefficient and the square of the speed. Doubling speed quadruples the drag force.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.204 • How an EV Performs — Modelling Basics
305What is the drag coefficient (Cd)?
Cd is a dimensionless number describing how slippery a vehicle's shape is in air. A typical saloon car has Cd around 0.3. Some EVs achieve as low as 0.19. Buses and motorcycles, with bluffer shapes, typically have Cd values closer to 0.7.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.204 • How an EV Performs — Modelling Basics
306What is hill-climbing force?
Hill-climbing force is the component of the vehicle's weight that acts along the slope when going up a hill. It equals the vehicle's weight times the sine of the slope angle. It opposes motion on uphills and helps motion on downhills (becoming negative).
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.204 • How an EV Performs — Modelling Basics
307What is linear-acceleration force?
By Newton's second law, accelerating a vehicle of mass m needs a force F = m × a. This is the linear-acceleration term in the tractive-effort equation. If the vehicle slows down, the term goes negative, meaning the wheels are doing braking work rather than driving work.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.204 • How an EV Performs — Modelling Basics
308Why must rotational acceleration of the motor be considered?
The motor's rotor must speed up too, not just the whole vehicle. Even if its moment of inertia is small, the motor spins at angular speeds much higher than the wheels (gear ratio amplifies). So accelerating it takes extra force, often added approximately as a 5% mass increase.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.204 • How an EV Performs — Modelling Basics
309What is the formula for total tractive effort?
Total tractive effort is the sum of rolling-resistance force (F_rr), aerodynamic drag (F_ad), hill-climbing force (F_hc), linear-acceleration force (F_la) and rotor-acceleration force (F_ωa). Slowing down or going downhill makes some terms negative. This sum drives all EV performance and range modelling.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.206 • How an EV Performs — Modelling Basics
310Why is acceleration to 30 or 50 kph the EV equivalent of 0–60 mph?
For IC cars, 0–60 mph is the standard acceleration test. EVs don't have a single agreed measure, but the closest equivalents are the 0–30 kph and 0–50 kph times. These shorter benchmarks are often used because many electric vehicles emphasise urban performance, not all-out top speed.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.206 • How an EV Performs — Modelling Basics
311How does motor torque vary with speed in performance modelling?
At low speeds the motor delivers a constant maximum torque T_max. Above a critical angular speed ω_c the torque falls. In a brushed DC motor it falls linearly with speed. In most brushless motors it falls in such a way that the output power stays constant.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.208 • How an EV Performs — Modelling Basics
312What is a driving cycle?
A driving cycle is a fixed pattern of vehicle speed against time, intended to mimic realistic driving. It is used in tests and simulations to measure range, fuel use or emissions under more realistic conditions than steady-state running. Many cycles exist for cities, highways and combined driving.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.214 • How an EV Performs — Modelling Basics
313What is the LA-4 driving cycle?
The LA-4 cycle was based on actual traffic flows in Los Angeles. It became one of the first widely used standardised driving cycles. It was later developed into the Federal Urban Driving Schedule (FUDS), a 1500-second cycle that gives each second a different target vehicle speed.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.214 • How an EV Performs — Modelling Basics
314What is the FUDS driving cycle?
FUDS, the Federal Urban Driving Schedule, is a US driving cycle simulating city traffic. It lasts 1500 seconds, with a different target speed for each second. There is also a simplified 360-second version (SFUDS) that keeps the same average speed and gives very similar range simulation results.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.214 • How an EV Performs — Modelling Basics
315What is the ECE-15 driving cycle?
The ECE-15 driving cycle is a European urban driving cycle, useful for testing small vehicles like battery electric cars. It uses simple periods of constant acceleration and constant velocity. In EC emission tests it is combined with the Extra-Urban Driving Cycle (EUDC), which has a maximum speed of 120 kph.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.216 • How an EV Performs — Modelling Basics
316What tools are commonly used for EV performance modelling?
Standard mathematical software such as MATLAB® and spreadsheets like Microsoft Excel make excellent platforms for EV performance simulations. No special programming skills are required — the equations of motion can be evaluated step by step over time to predict acceleration, top speed and range.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.201 • How an EV Performs — Modelling Basics
317What is the value of air density typically used?
Air density changes with temperature, altitude and humidity. For most EV calculations a reasonable working value is 1.25 kg/m³. Using SI units (frontal area in m², velocity in m/s) with this air density gives an aerodynamic drag force directly in newtons.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.204 • How an EV Performs — Modelling Basics
318What two types of range tests are used?
There are two main types: a constant-velocity simulation, where the car runs at one steady speed (unrealistic but simple), and a driving-cycle simulation, where speed varies continuously through a realistic city or highway profile. The second is more useful but needs more complex computation.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.214 • How an EV Performs — Modelling Basics
319Why does the simple constant-velocity range test still matter?
A constant-velocity range test ignores the reality of stop-start driving and traffic, but its rules are unambiguous. Every vehicle is tested the same way. So even though the absolute range numbers may be unrealistic, they give a useful basis for comparing one vehicle directly against another.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.214 • How an EV Performs — Modelling Basics
320What is the Federal Highway Driving Schedule (FHDS)?
FHDS is a US driving cycle used to simulate out-of-town highway driving. It is widely used but has a top speed seen as a little low for real highways. The newer US06 cycle, with a more realistic high-speed profile, is increasingly used in its place for highway tests.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.215 • How an EV Performs — Modelling Basics
321Why is gear-system efficiency included in the model?
Even a single-stage gear coupling is not 100% efficient. Typical values for an EV are around 95–98%. So the force at the wheels is slightly less than ideal. The model multiplies motor torque by gear efficiency η_g to give a more accurate prediction of vehicle acceleration.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.205 • How an EV Performs — Modelling Basics
322How is the motor's torque related to the force at the wheels?
Wheel torque equals tractive force times wheel radius r. The motor torque is connected to wheel torque by the gear ratio G. So tractive force at the wheels = G × T_motor / r, where T_motor is the motor's torque output and r is the tyre's radius.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.204 • How an EV Performs — Modelling Basics
323How does motor angular speed relate to vehicle speed?
If a vehicle moves at velocity v and the tyre has radius r, the axle turns at v/r radians per second. The motor spins at G times this — that is, motor angular speed = G·v/r. So a higher gear ratio means the motor spins faster than the wheels.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.205 • How an EV Performs — Modelling Basics
324Why is the GM EV1 a good benchmark for performance modelling?
The GM EV1 was the first modern electric car from a really large motor company. It had an ultra-low drag coefficient (0.19), low rolling resistance (0.0048) and used variable-frequency induction motors spinning up to nearly 12,000 rpm — all real published data that's useful for validating simulations.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.212 • How an EV Performs — Modelling Basics
325What does an EV acceleration simulation look like in practice?
The equations are usually solved in small time steps (e.g. 0.1 s). At each step the model computes the tractive effort, divides by mass to get acceleration, and updates velocity. Spreadsheets like Excel or scripts in MATLAB® make this very straightforward to set up and rerun with changes.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.209 • How an EV Performs — Modelling Basics
326What is the value of a 5% mass increase shortcut?
Sometimes the motor's moment of inertia I is unknown. A simple workaround is to ignore the angular-acceleration term entirely and instead pretend the vehicle is 5% heavier in the linear-acceleration term. This crude shortcut gives reasonable accuracy without needing to look up rotor inertia values.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.206 • How an EV Performs — Modelling Basics
327Why is range modelling more complex than top-speed modelling?
Top speed is reached in a steady-state balance of forces. But range requires summing energy used over an entire driving cycle, where every second has a different speed, acceleration, motor efficiency and battery loss. Computers handle the bookkeeping easily, but the model itself is much more involved.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.214 • How an EV Performs — Modelling Basics
328What does 'tyre radius' affect in the model?
The tyre radius r ties motor torque to wheel torque (F = G·T/r) and motor speed to vehicle speed (ω = G·v/r). Smaller wheels give more wheel torque for the same motor torque but spin faster, so they need higher motor speeds to reach the same road speed.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.204 • How an EV Performs — Modelling Basics
329What is 'critical motor speed' ω_c?
Critical motor speed ω_c is the rotational speed at which the motor's torque begins to fall. Below ω_c the motor produces a constant maximum torque T_max. Above it, the torque either drops linearly (brushed DC) or drops in proportion to 1/speed (most brushless motors) to keep power constant.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.208 • How an EV Performs — Modelling Basics
330Why is performance modelled at maximum torque, not maximum power?
For acceleration figures, you want the strongest pull at every speed. For an IC car that means running at maximum power. For an electric motor it means running at maximum available torque, since torque produces the wheel force that gives the vehicle its acceleration during the run-up.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.206 • How an EV Performs — Modelling Basics
331Why is aerodynamic drag the killer of high speeds?
Drag rises as the square of speed, while the power needed to overcome it rises as the cube of speed. So pushing the vehicle from 80 to 100 kph takes far more extra power than going from 30 to 50 kph. That's why streamlining matters most at speed.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.204 • How an EV Performs — Modelling Basics
332Why can EVs achieve lower Cd than IC cars?
Internal-combustion engines need large air intakes and pipes for radiators and exhausts. EVs have far less under-vehicle plumbing and can place big components like batteries more freely. That gives designers more freedom to streamline the body, so EVs can reach Cd values that IC cars struggle to match.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.204 • How an EV Performs — Modelling Basics
333Why do well-pumped bicycle tyres roll so easily?
Rolling resistance depends mainly on tyre hysteresis losses, which increase with tyre flex. A well-inflated tyre flexes less under load, so its hysteresis losses are smaller. Cyclists notice the resulting low μ_rr — the bike rolls freely — although the ride feels harsher with hard tyres.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.204 • How an EV Performs — Modelling Basics
334Why does the angular-acceleration term often turn out small?
For a typical 800 kg car with a 30 kW motor, the term I·G²/r² is about 40 kg — only 5% of the 800 kg vehicle mass. So the angular acceleration of the motor matters, but compared with linear acceleration of the whole car it's a small correction.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.206 • How an EV Performs — Modelling Basics
335Why is finite-difference numerical solution preferred in EV modelling?
Many EV motion equations don't have neat algebraic solutions. Finite differences just approximate dv/dt by (v_n − v_{n−1}) / Δt, letting you step forward in tiny time slices, computing one velocity from the previous one. This makes modelling easy to implement in Excel or MATLAB®.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.209 • How an EV Performs — Modelling Basics
336Why does an EV simulation get validated against real-car data?
Models are only useful if their numbers match reality. The textbook validates its method against the published 0–60 mph time of the GM EV1 (just under 9 seconds). When the simulated number matches the real one, you gain confidence in the underlying equations and constants.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.214 • How an EV Performs — Modelling Basics
337Why might driving cycles give very different range from the constant-speed test?
City driving cycles include lots of acceleration, deceleration and idling — each of which uses energy differently from steady running. So a constant-speed range estimate at 50 kph often greatly overestimates the actual urban range. Driving cycles produce a more realistic figure for how far the vehicle will actually go.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.214 • How an EV Performs — Modelling Basics
338Why do EV manufacturers care about the FUDS test result?
FUDS simulates typical city traffic where most EVs are used. Its result represents the kind of range customers will actually see in real urban driving. Other tests like FHDS or US06 measure highway range, while ECE-15 in Europe and US06 stand in for fast or steady cruising.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.214 • How an EV Performs — Modelling Basics
339Why is rotor inertia more important at high gear ratios?
Motor angular acceleration is amplified by gear ratio G when reflecting back to the wheels. The force needed to accelerate the rotor scales with G²/r² times the rotor's inertia. So with a high-ratio gearbox, even a small rotor inertia can become a noticeable acceleration load.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.205 • How an EV Performs — Modelling Basics
340Why are simulations useful even before a vehicle is built?
Simulations let designers try different motor powers, battery types, masses and aerodynamics before committing to expensive hardware. You can quickly see how each change affects acceleration and range — and pick the best compromise long before metal is cut. This saves cost and shortens development.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.201 • How an EV Performs — Modelling Basics
341How does the model handle a brushed-DC vehicle in the high-speed region?
Once the motor speed exceeds ω_c, the brushed DC motor's torque falls linearly with speed. The model substitutes this torque-speed relationship into the tractive-effort equation, giving a first-order differential equation for v that can be solved numerically with a finite-difference time step.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.208 • How an EV Performs — Modelling Basics
342Why does a brushless EV operate at constant power above ω_c?
Most brushless motors are controlled so that as speed rises beyond the critical value, torque is reduced inversely with speed, keeping the product T·ω (the power) constant. This 'constant-power region' lets the vehicle keep accelerating at high speed without exceeding the motor's safe current or thermal limits.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.208 • How an EV Performs — Modelling Basics
343How is the scooter example's 250 A current limit important?
Without a current limit, the motor would draw nearly 1500 A at stall, which would damage it. The controller imposes a safe 250 A maximum (or 350 A for up to 5 seconds), which in turn fixes the maximum torque T_max for the simulation at 34 N·m.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.208 • How an EV Performs — Modelling Basics
344How does the EV1 simulation switch behaviour at 71.3 kph?
Below 19.8 m/s (71.3 kph) the EV1 motor delivers its full 140 N·m. Above it, the motor moves into the constant-power region at 102 kW. The simulation tracks two different differential equations for the two regions and joins them when velocity reaches 19.8 m/s.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.212 • How an EV Performs — Modelling Basics
345How does the controller cap top speed in the EV1 model?
Once velocity reaches 35.8 m/s, the EV1 controller stops any further acceleration to protect the system. The simulation models this by detecting the cap and freezing speed there. So in the velocity-versus-time plot, the curve flattens off at the controller-set top speed.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.214 • How an EV Performs — Modelling Basics
346Why isn't a single number good enough for motor efficiency in range models?
Motor efficiency varies widely with speed and torque. Range simulations spend many seconds in low-efficiency zones (low load, low speed) so using a single peak efficiency overstates the energy available. A proper model uses an efficiency map or formula η(T,ω) at every step of the cycle.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.196 • How an EV Performs — Modelling Basics
347How is the rolling-resistance coefficient measured in practice?
μ_rr can be found by pulling the vehicle at a steady, very low speed (so aerodynamic drag and acceleration forces are negligible) and measuring the pulling force needed. Dividing that force by the vehicle's weight gives the rolling-resistance coefficient directly.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.204 • How an EV Performs — Modelling Basics
348Why is performance modelling sometimes done at WOT (wide open throttle)?
Wide-open-throttle tests simulate the maximum-power demand on the system, like a hard 0–60 launch. In an electric car this means asking for maximum available torque at every step. WOT runs reveal acceleration headroom and reproduce the official 0–60 figures published by manufacturers for direct comparison.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.212 • How an EV Performs — Modelling Basics
349Why is a small time step crucial in EV finite-difference simulations?
Vehicle dynamics change quickly, especially during acceleration. A small time step (e.g. 0.1 s) keeps the finite-difference approximation accurate enough to track velocity faithfully. Too large a step would over- or under-shoot the real curve. Too small a step just makes the spreadsheet slow.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.211 • How an EV Performs — Modelling Basics
350What can EV performance simulations reveal beyond top speed and range?
Simulations capture motor torque, speed, current and battery state at every moment. That lets designers optimise motor and gear selection, choose battery sizing, predict overheating risks, and balance trade-offs. The same data can drive component-level design decisions, not just headline 0–60 or range numbers.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.201 • How an EV Performs — Modelling Basics
351What is the ideal aerodynamic shape for a vehicle?
The ideal aerodynamic shape is a teardrop, like a free-falling water droplet. Its drag coefficient can be as low as 0.04 when its length-to-diameter ratio is 2.4. Real cars never get this low, but aerodynamicists treat the teardrop as the 'aiming point' for body design.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.233 • EV Design & Ancillary Systems
352What is a typical drag coefficient for a normal hatchback?
A typical saloon or hatchback car has a drag coefficient (Cd) between about 0.3 and 0.5. A reasonably aerodynamic van is around 0.5. The Honda Civic hatchback, for example, has Cd ≈ 0.36 with a frontal area of about 1.9 m².
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.233 • EV Design & Ancillary Systems
353What's the drag coefficient of the GM EV1 electric car?
The GM EV1 has an extraordinarily low drag coefficient of 0.19. The Honda Insight hybrid is also very low at 0.25, while the Bluebird record-breaking electric car achieved Cd ≈ 0.16 — all illustrating how aerodynamics matter even more for EVs than for petrol cars.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.233 • EV Design & Ancillary Systems
354How does lowering Cd from 0.30 to 0.19 affect range?
Drag — and the power used to overcome it — is directly proportional to Cd. Lowering Cd from 0.30 to 0.19 cuts drag to about 63% of the original. So battery capacity needed to overcome air drag drops by about 37%, or the vehicle range goes up significantly.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.233 • EV Design & Ancillary Systems
355What is the Kamm-back design?
The Kamm back is an aerodynamic trick used on cars like the Honda Insight. Beyond a certain point, tapering the rear of the body produces little extra benefit, so the body is simply cut off vertically. This avoids long, useless tail sections while keeping drag low.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.236 • EV Design & Ancillary Systems
356Why does the Honda Insight have narrower rear wheels?
On the Honda Insight, the rear track is 110 mm narrower than the front. This lets the body taper towards the back, approaching a teardrop shape. Narrower rear wheels plus a sloping floor and rear hatch together let the car neck in smoothly, reducing aerodynamic drag.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.236 • EV Design & Ancillary Systems
357Why are under-body covers used on EVs?
The under-body is a major source of turbulence. EVs and hybrids like the Honda Insight use flat under-body covers to smooth airflow beneath the vehicle, reduce drag and improve range. Separate fairings protect exhaust pipes and fuel tanks so air still flows smoothly around them.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.236 • EV Design & Ancillary Systems
358Does rolling resistance depend on vehicle speed?
No. Rolling resistance is independent of speed. It is set by the coefficient of rolling resistance and the vehicle's weight. Higher tyre pressure or better-designed EV tyres can lower the coefficient, but the force stays roughly the same at 20 kph or at 100 kph.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.238 • EV Design & Ancillary Systems
359Why is aerodynamic drag unimportant at low speeds?
Aerodynamic drag rises with the square of speed, so it is tiny at low speeds. Below about 50 kph, rolling resistance dominates and aerodynamics barely matter. Above 50 kph, aerodynamic drag rises quickly and becomes the major influence on the power needed to keep moving.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.238 • EV Design & Ancillary Systems
360Why don't EVs need a clutch or multi-gear gearbox?
An electric motor produces torque from zero speed upwards, so no clutch is needed to disengage it. A single fixed gear ratio is normally enough, because the motor's wide speed range covers everything from idling to top speed. This makes EV transmissions far simpler than IC engine ones.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.238 • EV Design & Ancillary Systems
361What are the three main EV transmission layouts?
Three main layouts exist: (a) one motor driving a pair of wheels through a differential, (b) one motor per wheel via a small gearbox (no differential needed), and (c) the motor mounted directly inside the wheel hub. Each trades cost, weight and complexity differently.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.238 • EV Design & Ancillary Systems
362What is an in-wheel motor?
An in-wheel motor is built directly into the hub assembly of a wheel, with no shaft, gearbox or differential between motor and wheel. It can offer 100% mechanical transmission efficiency. Popular in electric scooters and bicycles, it saves space but raises the wheel's mass.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.239 • EV Design & Ancillary Systems
363Why does extra vehicle mass matter so much in an EV?
Heavier vehicles have higher rolling resistance, climb hills less efficiently, and waste more kinetic energy starting and stopping in cities. In an EV, all of these mean bigger and more expensive batteries to keep the same range. So weight directly drives up the vehicle's cost.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.242 • EV Design & Ancillary Systems
364Why is hill climbing demanding for an EV?
Climbing a hill needs power proportional to the vehicle's weight and to the sine of the slope angle. A 1500 kg EV at 80 kph on a 10° slope needs about 12× the power used on the flat. That is why motors must be sized for the worst hills.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.242 • EV Design & Ancillary Systems
365What is regenerative braking's practical limit?
The maximum practical limit on the recovery of kinetic energy through regenerative braking is about 40%. In light vehicles the losses associated with starting and stopping are smaller anyway, so the benefit of regenerative braking is also smaller than in heavy cars on hilly or stop-start routes.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.243 • EV Design & Ancillary Systems
366What is a monocoque body?
A monocoque integrates body and chassis into a single shell where every part — including the glazing — contributes to strength and stiffness. This makes the vehicle much lighter than a separate body-on-frame chassis. Most modern cars use monocoque construction, though some EVs still use a separate chassis.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.244 • EV Design & Ancillary Systems
367What should an ideal vehicle chassis or body do?
It should be strong, light, rigid, vibration-free, aerodynamic, impact-resistant and able to crumple evenly in a crash to protect passengers. It must also be cheap, corrosion-proof, aesthetically pleasing, and provide hard points to which other components can be attached securely.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.244 • EV Design & Ancillary Systems
368How does EV layout differ from IC car layout?
An EV has no engine bonnet, fuel tank or exhaust system. Batteries can be placed anywhere along the bottom of the vehicle (often low, for a low centre of gravity). Motors and gearing can be in the hubs. This gives designers vastly more flexibility in vehicle layout.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.246 • EV Design & Ancillary Systems
369What is the Twike three-wheeler?
The Twike is a popular three-wheel electric commuter vehicle. It has one wheel at the front and two at the rear, with two passengers sitting side by side. The two rear wheels provide stability. Its low-speed urban use makes its 'wrong-way-round' aerodynamic shape acceptable.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.246 • EV Design & Ancillary Systems
370What is the SAE J227a driving schedule?
