The SAE J227a is an old (1970s) driving cycle developed specifically for EVs. It has four variants (A–D) with different speeds. Each cycle is short, consisting of acceleration, constant velocity, coast, braking and a stationary phase. SAE J227a-C is most often used for electric scooters and city EVs.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.217 • EV Design & Ancillary Systems
371What is the ECE-47 cycle used for?
The European ECE-47 cycle is used for emission testing of mopeds and motorcycles with engines under 50 cc. It is also widely used to simulate the range of electric scooters. Unlike fixed-speed cycles, ECE-47 runs the vehicle at wide-open throttle for 50 seconds before settling to 20 kph.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.217 • EV Design & Ancillary Systems
372How is EV range determined in a battery simulation?
At each 1-second step, the simulation finds tractive effort needed, computes motor power, applies motor efficiency, adds accessory load, then updates the battery's state of charge using its current and the Peukert correction. The process repeats through driving cycles until the battery hits 80% depth of discharge.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.219 • EV Design & Ancillary Systems
373How does cold weather affect EV range?
Cold weather raises battery internal resistance, increases the Peukert coefficient slightly and lowers cell voltage. In a GM EV1 simulation, switching from clement daytime to cold-and-dark conditions drops range from over 90 miles to about 70 — matching the manufacturer's '50–90 miles, depending on conditions'.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.224 • EV Design & Ancillary Systems
374Does regenerative braking help a scooter much?
On a small electric scooter, the textbook simulation shows that adding 50% motor braking only extends range from 48.8 km to about 51 km — barely 4%. So in a light vehicle the benefit of regenerative braking is small, though it is much greater for heavy or aerodynamic cars.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.224 • EV Design & Ancillary Systems
375Why is a fuel-cell vehicle simpler to simulate than a battery EV?
A fuel-cell vehicle has no regenerative braking to integrate, no Peukert current corrections, and no need to track battery state of charge. Fuel cell efficiency is essentially constant at all powers. So the simulation just tracks hydrogen used per second from the power demand.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.229 • EV Design & Ancillary Systems
376What is a hybrid vehicle's controller responsible for?
A hybrid controller monitors all power flows and battery state of charge, deciding moment by moment how much power should come from the engine, battery and electric motor. The controller's logic isn't fully determined by simple equations — it depends on operating conditions, history and need to equalise charge.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.230 • EV Design & Ancillary Systems
377What is ADVISOR?
ADVISOR (Wipke et al., 1999) is one of the best-known EV simulation programs. It is MATLAB-based and supports the modelling of hybrid electric vehicles, including their complex controller logic. The textbook recommends using Simulink rather than plain MATLAB for serious hybrid simulation.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.230 • EV Design & Ancillary Systems
378Why is the EV1 range better on SFUDS than at constant 120 kph?
Constant 120 kph asks the EV1 to fight maximum aerodynamic drag continuously, giving a range under 80 km. SFUDS spends time stopping and starting at speeds up to 60 kph — much lower drag — and the EV1 simulation shows over 140 km of range in good conditions.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.234 • EV Design & Ancillary Systems
379Why are wing mirrors and aerials worth redesigning in an EV?
Wing mirrors and antennas stick out of the body, creating turbulence and drag. Replacing them with electronic video systems and internal antennas — fully enclosed within the body's aerodynamic envelope — reduces drag. The smaller battery this enables can be cheaper than the added cost of cameras and electronics.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.235 • EV Design & Ancillary Systems
380Are differentials a good idea in EVs?
A differential is well-tested, reliable, mass-produced engineering, but it loses power and adds weight. EVs can sometimes eliminate the differential by using one motor per wheel with an electronic controller balancing the torques — at the cost of more complex electronics and (usually) higher per-kW motor cost.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.239 • EV Design & Ancillary Systems
381Why are aerodynamics more critical in EVs than in petrol cars?
In an IC car, slightly more drag just means burning a little more fuel — cheap. In an EV, extra drag means bigger or more batteries, which are heavy and expensive. So every reduction in Cd directly reduces battery cost or extends range, making aerodynamic refinement much more valuable.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.246 • EV Design & Ancillary Systems
382Why is in-wheel motoring tricky?
An in-wheel motor must run slowly (wheel speeds are 2–4× lower than typical motor speeds), which means a heavier motor. The added unsprung mass at each wheel also hurts handling. In-wheel motors save space and give 100% transmission efficiency, but cost more per kilowatt than centrally mounted motors.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.239 • EV Design & Ancillary Systems
383Why does an EV with the same Cd struggle more at high speed than at low speed?
Aerodynamic drag rises with the square of speed, and the power to overcome it rises with the cube. A 100 km range at 160 kph requires roughly 400 kg of lead-acid battery just to fight drag, even with a Cd of 0.19. So EVs are ill-suited to sustained high-speed driving.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.234 • EV Design & Ancillary Systems
384Why do golf buggies not need streamlining?
Golf buggies and similar very low-speed vehicles barely encounter aerodynamic drag — even at 30 kph it's tiny. The buggy's rolling resistance on grass dominates. So designing a teardrop body is wasted effort. On a saloon car driven at motorway speeds, by contrast, drag is huge and streamlining matters enormously.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.236 • EV Design & Ancillary Systems
385Why must hill climbing be folded into EV range tests?
Power needed to climb a 10° hill is much larger than for flat cruising — for a 1500 kg car, about 12× the flat power. Real journeys include hills, so simulations without them overstate EV range. The textbook suggests adding gradients to driving cycles for any specific route being modelled.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.242 • EV Design & Ancillary Systems
386Why is fuel cell efficiency roughly constant at all powers?
At low currents the fuel cell's voltage (and so efficiency) rises. But at low currents the auxiliary balance-of-plant — pumps, compressors, controllers — also takes a larger share of net output. The two effects roughly cancel, giving a fuel cell system net efficiency that is fairly flat across power levels.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.228 • EV Design & Ancillary Systems
387Why does a heavier car gain more from regenerative braking?
Heavier cars store more kinetic energy at any given speed (KE = ½ m v²). So they have more to recover when braking. Light vehicles never have much kinetic energy in the first place, so even recovering 40% of it yields little battery payback compared with the heavier case.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.243 • EV Design & Ancillary Systems
388Why is the GM EV1 motor so large compared to its average power use?
On the SFUDS urban cycle, the EV1 uses only about 12 kW average. But the motor is rated at 100 kW. The extra headroom is needed for hard acceleration and especially for climbing steep hills with a heavy car — peak demands that can be ten times the average usage.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.242 • EV Design & Ancillary Systems
389Why are vans harder to streamline than cars?
Vans need maximum interior volume to carry cargo, which favours boxy shapes. That conflicts with the slim, tapered teardrop shape that minimises drag. Designers use under-body covers, flat wheels and rounded corners to reduce Cd, but vans never reach the low values that purpose-built EV cars can.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.236 • EV Design & Ancillary Systems
390Why are low-rolling-resistance tyres especially valuable for low-speed EVs?
On slow vehicles like mobility scooters or golf carts, air drag is tiny and rolling resistance dominates. Cutting μ_rr from 0.015 to 0.005 cuts that loss to one-third. So battery mass can be tripled in range, or cut by two-thirds for the same range — a substantial saving.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.238 • EV Design & Ancillary Systems
391How does battery mass for aerodynamic drag scale with speed?
Battery mass to fight aerodynamic drag scales as Cd·A·v² × distance ÷ specific energy ÷ efficiency. So at 160 kph it takes roughly 4× the battery mass it would at 80 kph for the same range. That makes high-speed EVs particularly battery-hungry compared with low-speed city EVs.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.234 • EV Design & Ancillary Systems
392How does the EV1 fuel-cell conversion change vehicle mass?
Removing 594 kg of lead-acid batteries and adding 8.5 kg of hydrogen plus 51.5 kg of tank and 250 kg of fuel cell system gives a total vehicle mass of about 1066 kg, down from the original 1350-plus-kg EV1. Lighter total mass means less rolling and acceleration energy.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.229 • EV Design & Ancillary Systems
393How does the textbook compute the fuel-cell target operating voltage?
If the fuel-cell stack averages 0.65 V per cell but the system has 38% overall efficiency referred to LHV, then the equivalent 'system' voltage is about 0.475 V per cell. The 0.175 V gap represents power consumed by the balance-of-plant — pumps, compressors and controllers.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.228 • EV Design & Ancillary Systems
394What's the loss model used for motor efficiency in range simulation?
The model uses the same four-term loss formula as Chapter 7: copper losses k_c·T², iron losses k_i·ω, windage k_w·ω³, plus constant losses C. Plug torque T and angular speed ω at each second of the cycle into this formula to get current motor efficiency for that second.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.220 • EV Design & Ancillary Systems
395Why is a low centre of gravity a design goal in EVs?
Distributing heavy battery cells along the bottom of the vehicle lowers its centre of gravity, improving stability and handling. EVs have great flexibility here because batteries can be split and packed wherever convenient. This gives EVs a structural advantage IC cars don't easily match.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.246 • EV Design & Ancillary Systems
396How can you simulate constant-velocity range in MATLAB simply?
Use linspace(v, v, N) to create an array of N identical velocity values v. Drop this into the range-simulation in place of a real cycle. It's not elegant, but lets the same code base run constant-velocity tests using the cycle infrastructure already built. Useful for quick what-if checks.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.225 • EV Design & Ancillary Systems
397Why does the textbook prefer cycle-based simulation over constant velocity?
Constant-velocity tests ignore the stops, starts and varying speeds that dominate real driving. Driving cycles reflect actual usage patterns, including the time spent in low-efficiency regions of the motor map. So cycle results are more useful for range claims, even though they take more computation.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.225 • EV Design & Ancillary Systems
398Why are computational fluid dynamics (CFD) and wind tunnels used for EVs?
Real Cd values depend on every surface curve, gap and protrusion. CFD packages let designers predict drag from a 3D model before any prototype is built. Wind tunnels confirm CFD results on full-size cars, sometimes with rolling roads to mimic moving ground. Both are essential to optimise EV aerodynamics.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.238 • EV Design & Ancillary Systems
399Why does the EV's motor often spend time near its sweet spot?
Plotting motor operating points (torque vs angular speed) over a typical driving cycle often shows they cluster near 120 rad/s and 10 N·m — exactly the efficiency map's peak. So motor sizing has been well matched to the cycle, which helps explain why simulated range often beats the spec sheet.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.227 • EV Design & Ancillary Systems
400Why must hybrid simulation often be written in C, not just MATLAB?
A hybrid's energy-management controller is usually written in C for the real vehicle. Wrapping the simulation in the same language lets the actual controller code be tested directly in simulation. This reduces bugs and ensures the simulator and the production controller behave identically.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.230 • EV Design & Ancillary Systems
401What is well-to-wheels analysis?
Well-to-wheels analysis considers pollution and energy use across the whole chain that powers a vehicle — from extraction of the fuel or generation of the electricity at source, through any conversions, all the way to the energy actually delivered at the wheels. It avoids tunnel-vision focus on just the tailpipe.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.266 • Efficiency & Environmental Impact
402Why must 'efficiency' be carefully defined when comparing vehicles?
Efficiency only makes sense when comparing like with like. The same vehicle has different efficiency numbers depending on whether you count road energy versus electricity, road energy versus chemical fuel, or road energy versus energy at the original source — each is valid but tells a different story.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.264 • Efficiency & Environmental Impact
403What is the typical tank-to-wheels efficiency of an EV?
An EV's efficiency from electricity supplied to the battery through to energy delivered at the wheels is around 80%. By comparison, an IC vehicle from chemical fuel to wheels achieves only about 20%. So an EV uses far less stored energy for the same distance.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.264 • Efficiency & Environmental Impact
404How efficient is a typical coal power station?
An older coal power station has an efficiency around 25% (electricity generated divided by energy in the coal burnt). The current average is 28%. Modern power stations can reach 45%, and combined-cycle plants can achieve as high as 60%, which makes a big difference to well-to-wheels figures.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.264 • Efficiency & Environmental Impact
405How do you multiply efficiencies in a chain?
If a power station is 30% efficient, the lines that carry its power are 90% efficient, and the motor at the end is 80% efficient, the overall well-to-wheels efficiency is 0.30 × 0.90 × 0.80 = 0.216, or 21.6%. Each step multiplies the loss.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.264 • Efficiency & Environmental Impact
406How much CO₂ does a 100-mile EV journey cause in the UK?
A Nissan Leaf needs about 26.7 kWh of mains electricity (after 90% charging efficiency) to drive 100 miles. With UK grid CO₂ emissions of roughly 0.44 kg per kWh at the time of the book, that gives around 11.75 kg of CO₂ for the trip.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.264 • Efficiency & Environmental Impact
407How much CO₂ does the same 100-mile EV journey cause in France?
France's grid is largely nuclear and hydro, with CO₂ emissions around 0.07 kg per kWh — far below the UK's 0.44. The same 100-mile EV journey requiring 26.7 kWh of grid electricity therefore releases only about 1.87 kg of CO₂ in France.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.264 • Efficiency & Environmental Impact
408How much CO₂ does a 100-mile diesel journey produce?
A diesel car with 40 mpg fuel economy uses 11.25 litres of diesel to cover 100 miles. Each litre of diesel releases about 2.672 kg of CO₂ when burnt, so the total CO₂ release for the trip is roughly 30 kg, more than double a UK-charged EV.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.264 • Efficiency & Environmental Impact
409How does a fuel-cell car compare to a diesel for CO₂?
A Honda FCX fuel-cell car using hydrogen reformed from natural gas releases about 16.3 kg of CO₂ over 100 miles — roughly 54% of the diesel car's 30 kg. Honda claims around 60%. The fuel cell's advantage depends on how the hydrogen was made.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.264 • Efficiency & Environmental Impact
410Why does the source of hydrogen matter so much?
Hydrogen reformed from natural gas emits about 16.3 kg of CO₂ per 100 miles of FCX driving. Producing the same hydrogen by electrolysing water with UK grid power emits about 48.1 kg — even worse than diesel. Only renewable or nuclear electricity makes hydrogen truly low-carbon.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.265 • Efficiency & Environmental Impact
411What are the main pollutants from IC engine vehicles?
The major IC engine pollutants are carbon dioxide, carbon monoxide, nitrous oxides (NOx), volatile organic compounds (VOCs), particulate matter (PM) and sulfur dioxide. All cause documented health problems and contribute to urban air pollution. CO₂ is the main driver of climate change.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.270 • Efficiency & Environmental Impact
412How does carbon monoxide harm health?
Carbon monoxide inhibits the blood's ability to carry oxygen, which is particularly dangerous to smokers and people with heart disease. It can also cause permanent damage to the nervous system. So even sub-lethal exposure on busy streets is a real health concern.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.270 • Efficiency & Environmental Impact
413What harms do nitrous oxides (NOx) cause?
NOx exacerbate asthma, affect the lungs and make children and the elderly more vulnerable to respiratory infection. In sunlight with VOCs, NOx produce ground-level ozone, which irritates eyes and damages lungs. NOx also help form acid rain, which kills plants and fish.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.270 • Efficiency & Environmental Impact
414Why is particulate matter (PM) a serious health risk?
PM causes lung problems, worsens cardiovascular disease, damages lung tissue and can cause cancer. Ultra-fine PM gets past the upper airway, deep into lung tissue and the bloodstream. Above 5 μg/m³ it presents a real cancer risk and harms the reproductive and endocrine systems.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.270 • Efficiency & Environmental Impact
415What is the greenhouse effect of CO₂?
The sun's short-wave radiation is absorbed by the earth and re-emitted as longer-wavelength heat radiation. CO₂ and other greenhouse gases absorb that heat and re-radiate it back down, warming the earth's surface. Atmospheric CO₂ has risen by about 25% over the past 100 years.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.270 • Efficiency & Environmental Impact
416What share of UK energy use comes from road transport?
Road transport accounts for about 25% of total UK energy use, and therefore about 25% of the country's greenhouse-gas emissions. Other western countries show similar figures. So making transport more efficient — including the shift to EVs — can make a major dent in national CO₂ output.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.270 • Efficiency & Environmental Impact
417Why do petrol cars get less efficient in city traffic?
IC engines work poorly at low loads. In slow traffic, fuel consumption rises sharply per kilometre. EVs are different — motor efficiency dips a bit at low speeds, but battery efficiency rises, giving an overall fairly steady efficiency across all speeds. So EVs are much better suited to congested cities.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.270 • Efficiency & Environmental Impact
418What's the typical city traffic speed in London or Tokyo?
In cities such as London and Tokyo, the average traffic speed is normally less than 15 kph, and in rush hour it is considerably less. At these speeds, IC engine cars waste enormous amounts of fuel and emit much pollution, while EVs maintain a fairly steady, efficient operation.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.270 • Efficiency & Environmental Impact
419What are the simplest ways to clean up city traffic?
The simplest way to remove emissions from town and city streets is to enforce zero-emission vehicles by legislation. Even hybrid vehicles running on electricity in city zones (but petrol elsewhere) would massively cut the noise, particulates and NOx people breathe in dense urban environments.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.270 • Efficiency & Environmental Impact
420Are IC engines forever doomed to be polluting?
No. IC engines can run on sustainable fuels like Brazil's sugar-cane ethanol, or even on pure hydrogen with essentially zero exhaust pollutants. But it has turned out to be easier and more efficient to use fuel cells, and electricity for batteries can come from renewables — so EVs are winning.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.266 • Efficiency & Environmental Impact
421What is a heat pump used for in an EV?
A heat pump moves heat from one place to another, providing more heat energy out than electrical energy in. For every 1 kW of electricity, a heat pump typically delivers 3 kW of heat. The GM EV1 used a reversible heat pump for both cabin heating and cooling.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.256 • Efficiency & Environmental Impact
422Why is heating an EV trickier than heating a petrol car?
In an IC car, plenty of waste heat from the engine warms the cabin almost for free. In a pure battery EV, the motor and electronics produce little waste heat, so any cabin warmth must come from the battery — which directly reduces driving range.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.256 • Efficiency & Environmental Impact
423Why is electric power steering (EPS) better for an EV?
Hydraulic power steering is driven mechanically by the engine, placing a continuous load on it even when no steering assist is needed. EPS draws electric power only when assisting the driver, improving fuel/energy efficiency. It is also simpler and more reliable than a hydraulic system.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.259 • Efficiency & Environmental Impact
424Why do EVs sometimes use a stick controller instead of pedals?
A four-quadrant motor controller handles both acceleration and regenerative braking, so it is natural to use a single lever to control both. Pushing the stick forward accelerates the vehicle; pulling it back applies regenerative braking. Mechanical brakes are added only at the extreme of travel for safety.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.258 • Efficiency & Environmental Impact
425What can satellite navigation do for an EV beyond directions?
In an EV, satellite navigation can do more than route-find. It can keep journeys minimal, automatically direct drivers to nearby charging points, and enforce speed limits via the electronic motor controller. This improves road safety while maximising vehicle range — features that are easier in EVs than petrol cars.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.258 • Efficiency & Environmental Impact
426Why are low-rolling-resistance tyres popular on EVs?
Low-rolling-resistance tyres like the Michelin Proxima RR used on the GM EV1 reduce drag without compromising handling or safety. They are usually inflated to about 3.5 bar, giving a slightly firmer ride. The Proxima RR also has self-sealing tread, removing the need for a spare wheel.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.260 • Efficiency & Environmental Impact
427Why are luggage racks and external aerials bad for EV efficiency?
Roof racks, external aerials and wing mirrors all stick out into the airflow, increasing the drag coefficient and cutting EV range. Replacing wing mirrors with cameras, putting aerials inside rear windows and discouraging roof racks are all ways to keep the body's slippery aerodynamic profile intact.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.260 • Efficiency & Environmental Impact
428What's the role of selective-coated glass in EV thermal design?
Most cars act like greenhouses — sunlight enters through the glass and heats the interior. Glass coated with a selective layer reflects much of the solar radiation, sharply reducing internal heat build-up. That cuts the load on the air-conditioning system and so improves EV range.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.257 • Efficiency & Environmental Impact
429Why is pre-heating the cabin while charging a smart idea?
Heating or cooling the cabin half an hour before driving — using a remote control or timer while still plugged in — means the electricity for cabin comfort comes from the grid, not from the battery. The driver finds the cabin pre-conditioned without sacrificing range.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.257 • Efficiency & Environmental Impact
430What does an onboard charger do?
An onboard charger is a battery-charging unit carried inside the vehicle. Most EVs have one, so the car can be plugged into a regular wall socket anywhere when fixed external chargers aren't available. Onboard chargers usually charge more slowly than dedicated high-power roadside chargers.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.260 • Efficiency & Environmental Impact
431Why does an EV charged from solar power give a misleading 'efficiency' number?
A solar power station is only ~10% efficient end-to-end, so a solar-charged EV has a well-to-wheels efficiency of just 2.5%, ten times worse than a petrol car. But it still emits zero CO₂. The headline efficiency number on its own is misleading without considering carbon and cost.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.264 • Efficiency & Environmental Impact
432Why isn't the fuel-cell vehicle always greener than the EV?
If hydrogen comes from natural gas reforming, an FCX emits about 16 kg CO₂ per 100 miles — similar to a UK-charged battery EV. If hydrogen comes from electrolysis using UK grid power, an FCX emits 48 kg, worse than diesel. Only clean-electricity hydrogen makes fuel cells a real winner.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.266 • Efficiency & Environmental Impact
433Why does the EV's CO₂ advantage grow as the grid cleans up?
An EV's per-mile CO₂ scales with grid CO₂ per kWh. Today's UK average is about 0.44 kg/kWh; France's is 0.07. As grids add more nuclear and renewables, EV CO₂ falls — while a diesel car's emissions stay fixed. So EVs' lead over diesel widens every year.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.266 • Efficiency & Environmental Impact
434Why might using a small fuel-burning heater in an EV be a bad idea?
Fuel-burning cabin heaters were tried on US Postal Service EVs. They proved hard to control — drivers ended up opening doors to dump excess heat — and the vehicles consumed nearly as much fuel as the diesel vans they replaced. The EVs then no longer counted as zero-emission.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.256 • Efficiency & Environmental Impact
435How does heat storage in wax help an EV?
Heated wax stores energy via latent heat of fusion at a higher specific energy than a battery. Charged from the grid when the car is plugged in, it then warms the cabin on a cold morning without draining the battery. The car is warm by the time the driver gets in.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.256 • Efficiency & Environmental Impact
436Why can't sustainable IC engines simply replace EVs?
IC engines can run cleanly on hydrogen or sugarcane ethanol, but they still produce tail-pipe emissions (NOx) and noise locally. They are also far less efficient than electric motors. And renewable electricity scales more easily than producing huge volumes of sustainable liquid fuels, so EVs win out long-term.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.266 • Efficiency & Environmental Impact
437How does carbon fibre help cut EV battery cost?
Carbon fibre has by far the best strength-to-mass and rigidity-to-mass ratio of common chassis materials — nearly six times better than aluminium or steel. A carbon-fibre Formula One chassis can mass just 35 kg. Lighter chassis means smaller, cheaper battery for the same range, which can offset carbon's higher material cost.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.249 • Efficiency & Environmental Impact
438Why are crumple zones still essential in an EV body?
In a crash, a body absorbs energy via plastic deformation. A well-designed crumple zone forces this to happen progressively, spreading deceleration forces over more time and protecting occupants. Both metals and composites absorb impact energy, though metals' crush behaviour is easier to predict with modern finite-element software.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.249 • Efficiency & Environmental Impact
439Why is glass area both helpful and harmful in EVs?
Large glazed areas give passengers good visibility but also turn the cabin into an effective passive solar heater on hot, sunny days — quickly raising temperatures past safe limits. Selective coatings on glass can reflect much of the solar radiation, cutting cooling demand and improving range.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.257 • Efficiency & Environmental Impact
440Why is using the air-conditioner often greener than opening windows?
Opening windows at speed disrupts the body's airflow and substantially increases aerodynamic drag, shortening range. Keeping windows closed and running the air-conditioner can use less battery overall, especially on long high-speed drives. So full air-conditioning is recommended for EVs intended to do long distance.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.257 • Efficiency & Environmental Impact
441Why is 'fossil-fuel-to-wheels' efficiency about the same for petrol and EV (with coal grid)?
A petrol IC car turns ~20% of its fuel's chemical energy into wheel energy. An EV with 80% tank-to-wheels efficiency, charged from a 25% coal grid, lands at 0.25 × 0.8 ≈ 20% from fossil fuel to wheels. Locally cleaner, but globally similar — unless the grid gets cleaner.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.264 • Efficiency & Environmental Impact
442How would you compute a fuel-cell vehicle's well-to-wheels CO₂?
Start with the hydrogen consumed per 100 miles, then divide by reforming efficiency (70%) and compression efficiency (90%) to get natural-gas energy. Multiply by 0.185 kg CO₂ per kWh. For a Honda FCX using 1.67 kg of H₂, this gives 88.2 kWh and about 16.3 kg CO₂.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.264 • Efficiency & Environmental Impact
443What does a 25% rise in CO₂ over 100 years mean?
Atmospheric CO₂ has climbed about 25% in the last century, mostly from burning fossil fuels. Even small percentage rises trap a lot of extra heat globally. Effects include altered weather patterns in grain belts and melting ice caps that threaten coastal cities like London, New York and Perth.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.270 • Efficiency & Environmental Impact
444How does an EV compare to a diesel on a full UK 100-mile journey?
Diesel: ~30 kg CO₂. UK-charged EV: ~11.75 kg. So the EV saves over 60% of the diesel's emissions even on the UK's relatively dirty grid. The same EV in France emits about 1.9 kg — a 94% saving against diesel. So grid mix matters enormously.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.265 • Efficiency & Environmental Impact
445How does PM concentration above 5 μg/m³ matter?
Above about 5 μg per cubic metre of air, particulate matter — especially ultra-fine PM — presents a significant cancer risk. It also damages the reproductive and endocrine systems. Many PMs are confirmed mutagens. So city air pollution from diesel engines isn't just unpleasant; it is genuinely toxic.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.270 • Efficiency & Environmental Impact
446How does latent heat let water store more energy than batteries?
Storing 1 kg of insulated water heated 70°C above ambient holds 293 kJ (~81 Wh) — better than lead-acid (35 Wh/kg) or NiCad batteries. The ice form, melting from 0°C to 15°C, holds 110 Wh/kg via latent heat of fusion plus warming. Useful for cabin heating or cooling.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.256 • Efficiency & Environmental Impact
447Why is 3-nitrobenzanthrone a concerning find in diesel exhaust?
Researchers (Arlt, 2005) have isolated 3-nitrobenzanthrone in diesel exhaust. It is described as a highly potent mutagen — a compound that can damage DNA and seed cancer. Such findings underscore that diesel exhaust is far worse than just a smelly nuisance; it is genuinely hazardous to long-term human health.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.270 • Efficiency & Environmental Impact
448Why does the textbook give EV charging an extra 90% efficiency factor?
Charging is never 100% efficient: rectification, conversion and battery acceptance all lose some energy. The book assumes about 90% charging efficiency for the Nissan Leaf calculation, meaning a 24 kWh battery actually needs about 26.7 kWh from the wall to fill it.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.264 • Efficiency & Environmental Impact
449Why might legislation be the key driver of EV adoption?
Cleaner-but-pricier transport happens only when society makes individual people choose it. Laws and regulations can force the change — banning the dirtiest vehicles, taxing fossil fuels, requiring zero-emission zones. The textbook calls this 'collective coercion to be good' and sees it as central to scaling up EVs.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.270 • Efficiency & Environmental Impact
450How does electric bicycle availability help the environment beyond replacing cars?
If electric bicycles work well and are widely available, some drivers may abandon private cars altogether. That reduces emissions even more than replacing a petrol car with an EV — because a bicycle, electric or not, uses far less energy per kilometre than any car of any kind.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.266 • Efficiency & Environmental Impact
451What is CARB?
CARB stands for the California Air Resources Board. It is a Californian government body that sets vehicle emission rules. CARB pushed the use of catalytic converters on petrol-engine exhausts and later forced car makers to develop zero-emission electric vehicles for the California market.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.271 • Real EV Examples & Future
452What did California's CARB ask car makers to do in 1998?
In the late 1980s CARB ruled that car makers selling vehicles in California had to make 2% of their 1998 sales zero-emission vehicles, rising to 5% by the year 2000. The California car market is huge, so this pushed makers to develop electric vehicles seriously.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.271 • Real EV Examples & Future
453What is a Zero-Emission Vehicle (ZEV)?
A Zero-Emission Vehicle, or ZEV, is one that produces no exhaust emissions when driven, such as a battery electric vehicle. The California Air Resources Board used the ZEV idea to push car makers toward cleaner technology, even though well-to-wheel emissions also matter.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.271 • Real EV Examples & Future
454Why did California change its original zero-emission vehicle targets?
The original 1998 and 2000 ZEV sales targets proved too ambitious for car makers to meet. California also realised that hybrids and well-to-wheel analysis could give cleaner overall results than a small share of pure ZEVs, so CARB rolled back deadlines and added flexibility.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.272 • Real EV Examples & Future
455What is the average global efficiency of coal-fired power plants?
The average global efficiency of coal-fired power plants today is about 28%. That means only 28% of the energy in the coal becomes useful electricity. The most modern plants reach roughly 45%, and upgrading old plants is one easy way to cut carbon emissions.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.274 • Real EV Examples & Future
456What is the world's most efficient coal-fired power station?
Unit 3 at the Nordjylland Power Station in Denmark is currently the world's most efficient coal-fired power station. It is rated at 400 MWe and has reached 50% efficiency, almost double that of older coal plants, by using more advanced combustion and steam technology.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.274 • Real EV Examples & Future
457What is a combined-cycle power station?
A combined-cycle power station uses two or more heat engines in tandem, so heat that would otherwise be wasted by the first engine is captured and used by the second. This lifts overall efficiency above 50%, and the very best designs have reached 60%.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.274 • Real EV Examples & Future
458What are the two main ways of turning sunlight into electricity?
The two well-established ways are photovoltaic panels, which convert sunlight straight into DC electricity, and solar thermal systems, which use sunlight to heat a fluid that drives a steam turbine. Both can supply electricity to a grid that can later charge electric vehicles.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.275 • Real EV Examples & Future
459How does the amount of sunshine vary between Alaska and the Sahara?
Average sunshine on a flat surface is about 87 watts per square metre in Alaska but around 273 watts per square metre in the Sahara. So a solar panel placed in the desert collects roughly three times more energy than the same panel placed near the Arctic.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.275 • Real EV Examples & Future
460What efficiency do typical mass-produced solar panels reach?
Cheap, mass-produced solar photovoltaic panels turn about 10% of the sunlight that hits them into electricity. More expensive panels can reach about 20%. The cheaper, lower-efficiency type is expected to be the most widely deployed, because of its much lower cost per watt.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.275 • Real EV Examples & Future
461How might solar power help charge electric vehicles?
Solar power output peaks during the middle of the day. If EVs are scheduled to charge when solar electricity is most abundant, they soak up clean power that might otherwise be wasted. Linking solar farms in different time zones with HV DC lines can extend this benefit further.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.276 • Real EV Examples & Future
462What share of the world's electricity comes from hydroelectric power?
Hydroelectricity supplies about 6% of the world's electricity today. It is the most widely used form of renewable energy and accounts for roughly 21% of all renewable generation. Some countries depend on it heavily — for example, Canada gets about 60% of its electricity from hydro.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.277 • Real EV Examples & Future
463What is the Rance Power Station?
The Rance Power Station, on the Rance estuary in France, is the best-known tidal power scheme. It dams the estuary and channels rising and falling tides through 24 axial-flow turbines. It has an installed capacity of 240 MW and generates about 600 GWh of electricity per year.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.278 • Real EV Examples & Future
464What is the proposed Severn Barrage?
The Severn Barrage is a long-proposed tidal scheme in England that would dam the Severn estuary. A barrage at Weston-super-Mare would deliver 2 GW continuously. A larger dam further east could yield 36.5 TWh per year — roughly 15% of Britain's electric-transport requirement.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.279 • Real EV Examples & Future
465Where is the Gordon Dam, and how big is it?
The Gordon Dam is in Tasmania, Australia. It is one of many large hydro facilities used to generate electricity. The dam has an installed capacity of 430 MW, making it a meaningful contributor to renewable electricity supply on the island, while leaving local valley water levels regulated.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.278 • Real EV Examples & Future
466What is wave energy?
Wave energy is electricity generated by capturing the up-and-down motion of ocean surface waves. Several pilot devices have been built and tested, but none have yet reached large-scale commercial use. It remains an interesting future option for sustainable electricity supply in coastal countries.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.280 • Real EV Examples & Future
467What are biofuels?
Biofuels are liquid or solid fuels made from biomass — plant or animal matter. Examples include ethanol from fermented sugar, biodiesel from vegetable oil, and burning wood. Cars, ships, aircraft and trains can all run on biofuels using ordinary combustion engines instead of fossil fuels.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.281 • Real EV Examples & Future
468What is the main problem with using biofuels widely?
Biofuels work technically, but they compete with food crops for land. Supplying just today's aviation industry from biofuels would need an area roughly the size of Texas, Oklahoma, Kansas and Iowa combined. That land cost makes biofuels a poor replacement for fossil fuels at large scale.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.281 • Real EV Examples & Future
469What is nuclear fission?
Nuclear fission is the splitting of heavy atoms such as uranium to release large amounts of heat. That heat boils water to drive steam turbines that generate electricity. Fission produces no carbon dioxide, so it can power future electric transport, though radioactive waste must be managed.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.282 • Real EV Examples & Future
470What is a pebble-bed nuclear reactor?
A pebble-bed reactor is a nuclear-fission design in which uranium fuel is scattered through tennis-ball-sized graphite pebbles. Helium gas is used as the coolant rather than water, so the coolant cannot itself become radioactive. The design aims to overcome the safety problems of older reactors.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.282 • Real EV Examples & Future
471What is nuclear fusion?
Nuclear fusion is the joining of light atoms, like hydrogen, to release energy. It is the process that powers the sun and a hydrogen bomb. Researchers have produced fusion briefly in machines such as JET, but a commercial fusion power station is still many years away.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.282 • Real EV Examples & Future
472What does the book conclude about future zero-carbon power for transport?
The book concludes that electricity for transport can be produced without burning fossil fuels. The likely mix is nuclear fission plus a range of renewables, with solar energy as the main contributor, supplemented by wind, tidal and undersea-current generation, and possibly nuclear fusion in the long term.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.283 • Real EV Examples & Future
473What is a Low-Speed Vehicle (LSV)?
A Low-Speed Vehicle, or LSV, is a small electric vehicle aimed at short local trips like shopping, commuting and visiting friends. They typically have ranges below 100 km and are sold as eco-friendly transport for retirees and city users who do not need motorway speeds.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.287 • Real EV Examples & Future
474How far do most US drivers travel each day?
About 75% of US drivers are believed to make daily round trips of less than 40 km. That is well within the range of small electric Low-Speed Vehicles, which suggests LSVs could realistically replace petrol cars for most everyday driving, especially in rural areas with light traffic.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.287 • Real EV Examples & Future
475What kind of electric vehicle was the Peugeot 106 Electric?
The Peugeot 106 Electric and the Peugeot Partner were ordinary petrol Peugeots converted to run on batteries. Their range was good enough for city deliveries but shorter than purpose-built electric cars. They illustrate the cost-versus-range trade-off when converting an existing vehicle to electric drive.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.288 • Real EV Examples & Future
476What is the GM EV1?
The GM EV1 is a two-seat rechargeable-battery electric car launched by General Motors in 1997. Unlike the Peugeot, it was designed from scratch as an electric vehicle rather than converted from a petrol car. At launch it was probably the most advanced battery EV in the world.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.288 • Real EV Examples & Future
477What battery did the GM EV1 use first?
When the EV1 was first introduced in 1997 it used lead-acid batteries, specifically 26 valve-regulated high-capacity cells of 12 V and 60 Ah each. Later versions switched to nickel-metal-hydride (NiMH) batteries, which gave the car a longer driving range between charges.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.288 • Real EV Examples & Future
478What is the drag coefficient of the GM EV1?
The GM EV1 has a drag coefficient (Cd) of just 0.19, which at the time of its launch was the lowest of any production car. The slippery shape combined with very low rolling-resistance tyres reduces the power needed to push it through the air, extending its range.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.289 • Real EV Examples & Future
479What motor powers the GM EV1?
The EV1 is powered by a 102 kW three-phase AC induction motor. Power goes through a single-speed, dual-reduction gearset with a ratio of 10.946 to 1, so there is no clutch or multi-speed gearbox — typical of electric drivetrains where motor torque is available from rest.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.289 • Real EV Examples & Future
480How long does the GM EV1 take to charge?
Using its 220 V inductive charger, the EV1 takes about five and a half to six hours to charge from empty to full. Inductive charging means there are no exposed metal contacts, so the car can be charged safely outdoors in rain or other bad weather.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.289 • Real EV Examples & Future
481How is the GM EV1 unlocked and started?
The EV1 does not use a traditional metal key. The driver enters a five-digit personal identification code on a keypad outside the door to unlock the car, then enters the same code on a keypad on the centre console to switch the car on and drive away.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.291 • Real EV Examples & Future
482What is the top speed of the GM EV1?
The GM EV1 has an electronically limited top speed of 80 mph, which is about 128 kph. The car comes with traction control, cruise control, anti-lock brakes and airbags, so although it is a small two-seat electric car, it is equipped much like a regular production car.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.290 • Real EV Examples & Future
483What is the Nissan Leaf?
The Nissan Leaf is a five-door hatchback battery electric car launched commercially by Nissan. It uses a lithium-ion battery and a permanent-magnet synchronous motor, giving a range of about 100 miles (160 km) between charges. It was released in Japan in 2010 and later built in Britain.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.292 • Real EV Examples & Future
484How long does the Nissan Leaf take to charge?
From a home charging unit the Leaf takes about eight hours to charge from empty to full. A rapid charger can top it up to 80% of full capacity in just 30 minutes, which is handy for longer trips that need a short pause partway through.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.292 • Real EV Examples & Future
485What is the Honda Insight?
The Honda Insight is a hybrid passenger car that combines a small petrol engine with an electric motor powered by a battery. Both can drive the wheels. The Insight is not plugged in — instead, the engine and regenerative braking keep the battery charged as the car is driven.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.293 • Real EV Examples & Future
486Why does the GM EV1 have its rear wheels closer together than its front wheels?
Bringing the rear wheels in by about 23 cm gives the body a teardrop shape. A teardrop is one of the most aerodynamic shapes possible, so the trick reduces drag, lets a smaller motor push the car the same distance, and stretches the battery range — important for any EV.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.290 • Real EV Examples & Future
487Why was the GM EV1 only leased, never sold outright?
GM only leased the EV1 because it was made in limited numbers as a compliance car for California's ZEV rule. Once California rolled back the strict zero-emission mandate, GM pulled the car and recovered every lease, ending the programme rather than supporting long-term retail ownership of the car.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.289 • Real EV Examples & Future
488Why would rooftop solar alone not be enough to power future electric transport?
Rooftop solar panels only cover a small total area and only generate during daylight. Even if all suitable roofs were used, the output would only be a fraction of what mass EV charging would need. Big solar farms or other renewables would be required to fill the gap.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.275 • Real EV Examples & Future
489Why are biofuels not a real long-term replacement for petrol or diesel?
Biofuels can technically run any vehicle, but growing enough biomass to replace petrol and diesel competes with food production. Even matching just today's aviation fuel use needs roughly the cropland of Texas, Oklahoma, Kansas and Iowa combined, so biofuels cannot scale up without starving people.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.281 • Real EV Examples & Future
490Why does the Honda Insight count only as a partial zero-emission vehicle?
The Insight has an engine and a battery-driven motor. In heavy slow traffic it runs from the battery and emits nothing locally, behaving like a pure EV. At higher loads the petrol engine fires up, so it still emits exhaust gases — it is therefore only partly emission-free.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.293 • Real EV Examples & Future
491Why are globally distributed solar farms with HV DC links seen as attractive?
If solar farms are spread across longitudes and joined by high-voltage DC transmission, sunshine somewhere on the network is producing power at any hour of day. That smooths the supply, removing the day-and-night problem of a single site and giving steady electricity for EVs around the clock.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.276 • Real EV Examples & Future
492Why is undersea current energy more predictable than wind or solar?
Ocean currents are driven by tides and large-scale circulation, which follow regular astronomical and oceanographic patterns. Their speed and direction can be forecast accurately, while wind and sunshine depend on weather. That predictability makes underwater turbines a steady source of energy for a power grid.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.280 • Real EV Examples & Future
493Why did CARB switch to a credit-based system for zero-emission vehicles?
Strict ZEV-only rules ignored the fact that some hybrids may pollute less than a battery car charged from a coal-fired grid. A credit system lets a car maker earn fractions of a ZEV credit for selling cleaner-than-average vehicles, which better reflects true well-to-wheels emissions.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.272 • Real EV Examples & Future
494Why is a purpose-built EV better than converting a petrol car?
A car designed from scratch as an EV, like the GM EV1, can use slippery aerodynamics, low-rolling-resistance tyres and battery placement made specifically for an electric drive. A converted petrol car carries spare weight and shape, so it ends up with less range for the same battery size.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.291 • Real EV Examples & Future
495Why is the market for Low-Speed Vehicles expected to grow in western countries?
Western countries have ageing populations. Many active retired people still want to visit friends, shop and run errands, but do not need to drive long distances or at motorway speeds. Quiet, simple, short-range electric LSVs match this lifestyle, so demand is likely to rise steadily.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.287 • Real EV Examples & Future
496How does the Toyota Prius split power between its engine, motor, generator and wheels?
The Prius uses a planetary gearset called the power splitter and an electronically controlled CVT. The split sends engine power partly to the wheels and partly to a generator that charges the battery and feeds the motor. A dashboard display shows energy flow at every moment in real time.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.295 • Real EV Examples & Future
497How does the Chevrolet Volt switch from pure-electric to series-hybrid driving?
The Volt runs as a pure EV from its lithium battery for the first 40 to 80 km. Once the battery falls to a preset low level, the petrol engine starts but only drives a generator, which feeds the traction motor — making the Volt operate as a series hybrid.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.296 • Real EV Examples & Future
498How does the Honda Insight recover energy when the driver brakes?
On a light brake press, the Insight's motor/generator switches into generator mode. It loads the wheels and slows the car, turning kinetic energy back into electricity stored in the battery. Only when the driver presses harder do the conventional friction brakes engage to bring the car to a stop.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.293 • Real EV Examples & Future
499How much of Britain's electric-transport demand could the Severn Barrage cover?
A Severn Barrage near Weston-super-Mare would generate 2 GW continuously. A larger dam further east would double that and yield about 36.5 TWh per year — roughly 15% of all the electricity Britain would need to electrify its transport, from a single tidal site.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.279 • Real EV Examples & Future
500What efficiency gap separates old coal stations from modern combined-cycle stations?
Old coal-fired power stations averaged around 28% efficiency — only about a quarter of the fuel's energy reached the grid. Modern combined-cycle stations, which use two heat engines in tandem, regularly exceed 50%, with the best designs reaching 60% — roughly doubling the useful output per unit of fuel.
Electric Vehicle Technology Explained, 2nd Ed. (Larminie & Lowry, Wiley), p.274 • Real EV Examples & Future
501What is a Hybrid Electric Vehicle (HEV)?
A Hybrid Electric Vehicle combines an internal combustion engine and an electric motor within the same drive train. The electric motor usually supports the engine to improve fuel economy or performance, and the vehicle is propelled by either the engine or the electric drive.
Hybrid and Electric Vehicles Freebook (compilation of selected chapters), p.7 • Hybrid & Electric Vehicles Reference (CRC)
502What distinguishes a Plug-in Hybrid Electric Vehicle from a regular hybrid?
A Plug-in Hybrid Electric Vehicle carries a larger battery than a normal hybrid and can be recharged from home outlets or charging stations. Many PHEVs can run in fully electric mode if enough energy is stored in the battery, unlike standard hybrids.
Hybrid and Electric Vehicles Freebook (compilation of selected chapters), p.7 • Hybrid & Electric Vehicles Reference (CRC)
503Why are Full Electric Vehicles not truly zero-emission?
While Full Electric Vehicles release no pollutants while driving, the electricity used to recharge their batteries usually comes from power plants that do emit pollutants. So overall emissions depend on the cleanliness of the country's power generation, not just the vehicle itself.
Hybrid and Electric Vehicles Freebook (compilation of selected chapters), p.7 • Hybrid & Electric Vehicles Reference (CRC)
504What is range anxiety in the context of electric vehicles?
Range anxiety refers to driver worry about an EV's limited range plus its unpredictable depletion when heating, air conditioning, or detours are added. Because EV ranges below 300 km in good conditions are common, drivers fear running out of charge before reaching their destination.
Hybrid and Electric Vehicles Freebook (compilation of selected chapters), p.11 • Hybrid & Electric Vehicles Reference (CRC)
505Why has the cost of batteries been such a big obstacle to EV adoption?
Batteries make up a major portion of an EV's total price, keeping vehicles expensive even with government subsidies. Some companies have tried leasing batteries to customers to offset this cost. Forecasts suggest battery costs will fall dramatically in coming years, easing the pressure.
Hybrid and Electric Vehicles Freebook (compilation of selected chapters), p.11 • Hybrid & Electric Vehicles Reference (CRC)
506What is regenerative braking in an electric machine?
Regenerative braking is the braking mode in an electric machine where, instead of wasting kinetic energy as heat, the machine converts the vehicle's mechanical energy from the wheels back into electrical energy. This recovered energy can recharge the battery and help extend driving range.
Hybrid and Electric Vehicles Freebook (compilation of selected chapters), p.16 • Hybrid & Electric Vehicles Reference (CRC)
507Why does an electric motor typically give a vehicle better acceleration than an internal combustion engine?
Electric motors deliver full torque even at very low speeds, and their instantaneous power can be two or three times their rated power. This means an electric drive gives strong launch and excellent acceleration from a relatively small, nominally rated motor.
Hybrid and Electric Vehicles Freebook (compilation of selected chapters), p.16 • Hybrid & Electric Vehicles Reference (CRC)
508Why have DC series motors become obsolete in modern EV applications?
Although DC series motors matched road load characteristics well and were easy to control in older prototype EVs, their large physical size and heavy maintenance requirements (mostly due to brushes and commutators) have made them obsolete in the automotive industry and in motor drives generally.
Hybrid and Electric Vehicles Freebook (compilation of selected chapters), p.16 • Hybrid & Electric Vehicles Reference (CRC)
509What is a brushless DC motor?
A brushless DC motor is a permanent magnet motor with concentrated three-phase windings whose air-gap flux distribution is trapezoidal. It is driven by a six-switch inverter, much like an induction motor, but its control is simpler because of the trapezoidal back-EMF shape.
Hybrid and Electric Vehicles Freebook (compilation of selected chapters), p.16 • Hybrid & Electric Vehicles Reference (CRC)
510How do switched reluctance motors produce torque differently from DC or PM motors?
DC and permanent magnet motors produce torque by mutual interaction between two orthogonal magnetic fields. Switched reluctance motors instead use a varying-reluctance flux path: the rotor is pulled toward whichever position minimises the magnetic circuit's reluctance, giving torque without rotor windings or magnets.
Hybrid and Electric Vehicles Freebook (compilation of selected chapters), p.16 • Hybrid & Electric Vehicles Reference (CRC)
511Why are switched reluctance motors attractive for vehicle traction despite their drawbacks?
SR motors have a simple rotor with no windings, magnets, or cage, which gives them excellent fault tolerance, a high torque-to-inertia ratio, and the widest possible constant-power speed range. These traits are ideal for traction, though acoustic noise and torque ripple remain known concerns.
Hybrid and Electric Vehicles Freebook (compilation of selected chapters), p.16 • Hybrid & Electric Vehicles Reference (CRC)
512In an electric machine, what is the back-EMF?
The back-EMF, also called motional voltage, is the voltage induced in the armature when its conductors move through the magnetic field. For a DC machine, back-EMF is proportional to rotor speed when the field flux is held constant by design.
Hybrid and Electric Vehicles Freebook (compilation of selected chapters), p.22 • Hybrid & Electric Vehicles Reference (CRC)
513How is a separately excited DC motor defined in the CRC reference?
A separately excited DC motor is one whose armature and field windings are supplied by independently controlled DC sources. This independence allows separate control of armature current and field current, giving the maximum flexibility for tuning torque and speed in the machine.
Hybrid and Electric Vehicles Freebook (compilation of selected chapters), p.24 • Hybrid & Electric Vehicles Reference (CRC)
514Why is a DC series motor good for fast starting acceleration?
In a series DC machine the armature and field windings are in series, so the same current passes through both. This produces a very high starting torque, which translates into rapid acceleration. The trade-off is the loss of independent control over flux and armature current.
Hybrid and Electric Vehicles Freebook (compilation of selected chapters), p.24 • Hybrid & Electric Vehicles Reference (CRC)
515What does it mean to minimise losses in a separately excited DC motor?
Losses in a separately excited DC motor are minimised when the armature-circuit losses (mainly resistive copper losses in the armature) equal the field-circuit losses. The controller can pick armature voltage and field flux to balance those two loss terms, maximising efficiency at the chosen operating point.
Hybrid and Electric Vehicles Freebook (compilation of selected chapters), p.28 • Hybrid & Electric Vehicles Reference (CRC)
516Why does moving the armature winding into the stator simplify AC machines?
In AC machines the armature circuit sits in the stationary stator instead of the rotor, so commutators and brushes (needed in DC machines to flip rotor current) are no longer required. This eliminates brush wear and the electromagnetic interference associated with mechanical commutation.
Hybrid and Electric Vehicles Freebook (compilation of selected chapters), p.29 • Hybrid & Electric Vehicles Reference (CRC)
517What is synchronous speed in a three-phase AC machine?
Synchronous speed is the rotational speed at which the stator's three-phase mmf wave rotates around the air gap. It equals the angular frequency of the applied stator voltage, and the resulting mmf has a constant peak amplitude that circles the stator at this speed.
Hybrid and Electric Vehicles Freebook (compilation of selected chapters), p.39 • Hybrid & Electric Vehicles Reference (CRC)
518What is a squirrel-cage induction motor?
A squirrel-cage induction motor has a rotor made of short-circuited copper or aluminium bars set axially around stacked laminations, connected at each end by end rings shaped like a squirrel cage. Its rugged construction and low cost make it the workhorse of industry.
Hybrid and Electric Vehicles Freebook (compilation of selected chapters), p.42 • Hybrid & Electric Vehicles Reference (CRC)
519What is slip in an induction motor?
Slip is the difference between the rotor speed and the stator's synchronous speed, expressed as a fraction of the synchronous speed. The rotor must spin slightly slower than the field for the rotor bars to cut flux, induce a voltage, and produce torque.
Hybrid and Electric Vehicles Freebook (compilation of selected chapters), p.43 • Hybrid & Electric Vehicles Reference (CRC)
520Why is an induction motor usually run at small slip values?
Operating at small slip keeps the rotor frequency low, so the rotor leakage reactance has little effect and the motor stays in the linear part of its torque-speed curve. This avoids the heavy losses, voltage drops, and heating that come with high-slip operation.
Hybrid and Electric Vehicles Freebook (compilation of selected chapters), p.45 • Hybrid & Electric Vehicles Reference (CRC)
521How much vehicle range can regenerative braking typically add?
Regenerative braking can extend the driving range of an electric vehicle by roughly 10 to 15 percent. Not all of the kinetic energy is captured because sudden stops would demand power far higher than the propulsion motor's rating, so mechanical brakes are still required.
Hybrid and Electric Vehicles Freebook (compilation of selected chapters), p.52 • Hybrid & Electric Vehicles Reference (CRC)
522When does an induction machine work as a generator during braking?
An induction machine acts as a generator when its rotor turns faster than the synchronous speed, giving it a negative slip. The induced rotor currents reverse polarity and the electromagnetic torque becomes negative, opposing rotation and decelerating the vehicle while feeding energy back.
Hybrid and Electric Vehicles Freebook (compilation of selected chapters), p.52 • Hybrid & Electric Vehicles Reference (CRC)
523What is a four-quadrant drive in EV motor control?
A four-quadrant drive is a motor controller that can deliver positive or negative torque at positive or negative shaft speed. This lets the same machine run forward or in reverse and switch smoothly between motoring and regenerative braking modes, which EVs and HEVs always require.
Hybrid and Electric Vehicles Freebook (compilation of selected chapters), p.52 • Hybrid & Electric Vehicles Reference (CRC)
524Why do permanent magnet machines avoid the rotor copper losses found in induction motors?
In a PM machine the rotor field comes from permanent magnets rather than from coil currents. Because no rotor windings need to be energised, there is no resistive (copper) loss on the rotor, and there is no separate field exciter circuit to maintain.
Hybrid and Electric Vehicles Freebook (compilation of selected chapters), p.54 • Hybrid & Electric Vehicles Reference (CRC)
525What are the three common types of magnets used in PM machines?
Permanent magnet motors typically use ferrites, samarium cobalt, or neodymium-iron-boron magnets. Ferrites are cheap but weak; samarium cobalt gives much higher flux density at higher cost; neodymium-iron-boron offers the strongest residual flux density (1.1 to 1.25 tesla) but is sensitive to temperature.
Hybrid and Electric Vehicles Freebook (compilation of selected chapters), p.54 • Hybrid & Electric Vehicles Reference (CRC)
526Why is the interior PM machine well suited to EV applications needing flux weakening?
In an interior PMSM the magnets are buried inside the rotor, so the quadrature-axis inductance can be much larger than the direct-axis inductance. This larger difference enables flux-weakening operation, giving a wide constant-power speed range and reducing the need for multiple transmission gears.
Hybrid and Electric Vehicles Freebook (compilation of selected chapters), p.60 • Hybrid & Electric Vehicles Reference (CRC)
527What distinguishes a PM brushless DC motor from a PMSM?
PM brushless DC motors have trapezoidal back-EMF waveforms produced by concentrated stator windings, while PMSMs use sinusoidally distributed windings and have sinusoidal back-EMFs. The BLDC motor only needs six discrete rotor positions per electrical revolution, simplifying its control compared to the PMSM.
Hybrid and Electric Vehicles Freebook (compilation of selected chapters), p.62 • Hybrid & Electric Vehicles Reference (CRC)
528Why do PMSMs require a high-resolution position sensor?
A PMSM produces smooth torque by precisely shaping the stator currents so they stay aligned with the rotor magnet field. That alignment requires accurate knowledge of rotor angle, so a high-resolution position sensor (and current sensors) feed information back to the digital controller.
Hybrid and Electric Vehicles Freebook (compilation of selected chapters), p.57 • Hybrid & Electric Vehicles Reference (CRC)
529What are the three regions of an SRM's torque-speed envelope?
An SRM drive has a constant torque region below the base speed (current and torque limited), a constant power region where conduction angle is increased as speed rises, and a high-speed natural region where torque falls roughly as one over speed squared.
Hybrid and Electric Vehicles Freebook (compilation of selected chapters), p.73 • Hybrid & Electric Vehicles Reference (CRC)
530What is aerodynamic drag on a vehicle composed of?
Aerodynamic drag on a vehicle is made of two parts: shape drag, due to high-pressure zones in front and low-pressure zones behind the body, and skin friction, caused by shear stress in the boundary layer. Shape drag accounts for over 90 percent of total drag.
Hybrid and Electric Vehicles Freebook (compilation of selected chapters), p.81 • Hybrid & Electric Vehicles Reference (CRC)
531Why does a rolling tyre create a retarding force on a moving vehicle?
When a tyre rolls, hysteresis in the rubber makes the contact patch's normal pressure higher in front and lower behind. That shifts the road's reaction force ahead of the wheel centre, creating a moment that opposes the wheel's motion - the rolling resistance.
Hybrid and Electric Vehicles Freebook (compilation of selected chapters), p.81 • Hybrid & Electric Vehicles Reference (CRC)
532What is gradeability for a vehicle?
Gradeability is the maximum slope that a vehicle can start climbing from a standstill while all of its wheels remain on the gradient. It depends on the available tractive effort versus the combined resistance forces acting on the vehicle at start-up.
Hybrid and Electric Vehicles Freebook (compilation of selected chapters), p.84 • Hybrid & Electric Vehicles Reference (CRC)
533Why is proper brake balance important when designing a regenerative braking system?
Maximum braking deceleration is only achieved when all four tyres reach the peak friction level at the same time. If the front or rear axle locks up first, those wheels lose cornering grip, the vehicle becomes unstable, and the full braking potential is wasted.
Hybrid and Electric Vehicles Freebook (compilation of selected chapters), p.84 • Hybrid & Electric Vehicles Reference (CRC)
534How much of total braking force may sit on the front axle in a high-performance car?
In high-performance vehicles, as much as 80 percent of the braking force can act on the front axle. This concentration on the front wheels creates a much larger opportunity to recapture energy at the front when designing a regenerative braking system, compared with a rear-wheel layout.
Hybrid and Electric Vehicles Freebook (compilation of selected chapters), p.84 • Hybrid & Electric Vehicles Reference (CRC)
535Why is a continuously variable transmission attractive for fuel economy?
A CVT provides an effectively infinite number of gear ratios within its range, so the engine can be held at its most economical speed across a wide span of vehicle speeds. That lets the powertrain track an ideal torque-speed profile that fixed-ratio gearboxes cannot match.
Hybrid and Electric Vehicles Freebook (compilation of selected chapters), p.88 • Hybrid & Electric Vehicles Reference (CRC)
536Why combine a battery with an ultracapacitor in an EV energy storage system?
A battery provides high energy density but cannot easily handle sharp bursts of current. Pairing it with an ultracapacitor lets the capacitor supply or absorb high-power transients during acceleration and braking, reducing battery stress, extending battery life, and improving overall energy capture.
Hybrid and Electric Vehicles Freebook (compilation of selected chapters), p.92 • Hybrid & Electric Vehicles Reference (CRC)
537What is battery C-rate?
The C-rate is a parameter expressing how intensely a battery is being discharged relative to its capacity. Low C-rates tend to lengthen battery life span, so designers try to avoid instantaneous high-power charge or discharge pulses, which raise stress, heat, and internal resistance.
Hybrid and Electric Vehicles Freebook (compilation of selected chapters), p.92 • Hybrid & Electric Vehicles Reference (CRC)
538What is the main drawback of the passive parallel battery and ultracapacitor topology?
In the passive parallel configuration the battery and ultracapacitor share a bus with no controller between them, so the battery cannot have its current shaped or limited. Whenever the ultracapacitor draws or supplies current, the battery follows the same profile for voltage equalisation, increasing battery stress.
Hybrid and Electric Vehicles Freebook (compilation of selected chapters), p.92 • Hybrid & Electric Vehicles Reference (CRC)
539Why is the parallel-connected multi-converter topology said to be the most flexible?
Because each storage device has its own bidirectional DC/DC converter, the battery and ultracapacitor voltages are fully decoupled from the DC link and from each other. Power flows and controls are independent, which improves stability, efficiency, and reliability since one source can keep operating if another fails.
Hybrid and Electric Vehicles Freebook (compilation of selected chapters), p.95 • Hybrid & Electric Vehicles Reference (CRC)
540How does a flywheel energy storage system store energy?
A flywheel stores energy kinetically as a large rotating disk with a very high moment of inertia, spinning between roughly 20,000 and 50,000 rpm. A motor-generator either speeds up the flywheel to store energy or is driven by it to release energy when needed.
Hybrid and Electric Vehicles Freebook (compilation of selected chapters), p.103 • Hybrid & Electric Vehicles Reference (CRC)
541Why must superconducting magnetic energy storage operate at very low temperatures?
SMES coils only act as superconductors and avoid ohmic losses when chilled to very low temperatures. A cryogenic refrigeration system is therefore required, and its energy use and complexity cut the overall efficiency, which is a serious obstacle to using SMES at vehicle scale.
Hybrid and Electric Vehicles Freebook (compilation of selected chapters), p.106 • Hybrid & Electric Vehicles Reference (CRC)
542Among the simulated battery/UC topologies, which gave the lowest DC link voltage ripple?
The parallel-connected multi-converter topology gave the smallest DC-link voltage ripple, with a maximum of 400.7 V and minimum of 397.6 V (about 0.8 percent). The passive parallel and cascaded converter topologies each saw roughly 2.4 to 2.5 percent ripple over the same drive cycle.
Hybrid and Electric Vehicles Freebook (compilation of selected chapters), p.118 • Hybrid & Electric Vehicles Reference (CRC)
543What is a solar powered charging station?
A solar powered charging station, or SPCS, is an EV charging site whose electricity is generated by solar panels. The panels typically also shade the parking area, and most installations are tied to the local electrical grid so power is available even when sunlight is not.
Hybrid and Electric Vehicles Freebook (compilation of selected chapters), p.126 • Hybrid & Electric Vehicles Reference (CRC)
544Why are parking lots a particularly suitable place to install solar panels?
There are far more parking spaces than vehicles in the United States, and many spaces sit empty across stadiums, churches, shopping centres, and workplaces. Covering these unused spaces with panels adds clean generation, provides shade, and could supply a meaningful share of national electricity.
